The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Definition and Examples of Begging the Question
Defines and illustrates the fallacy of begging the question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of begging the question in syllogistic arguments and construct both simple and composite examples.

Core Idea
Begging the question is using the unknown conclusion itself as a premise in its own proof.

In Islamic logic, a valid demonstration must not assume what it is trying to prove. The fallacy of begging the question (al-musādaratu 'alā al-maṭlūb) occurs when the sought conclusion is taken as a premise, either directly or through a synonym. The author defines this fallacy and illustrates it with simple and composite categorical syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The author states that begging the question is taking the unknown sought conclusion (maṭlūb) itself as a premise, either by changing a term to a synonym or without change. The unknown must be proven by what is more apparent.
The fallacy is distinct from other types of incomplete demonstration.
2
Identify simple begging
In a simple syllogism, one of the premises is essentially the same as the conclusion. Example: 'Every human is a human; every human is laughing; therefore every human is laughing.' Here the major premise is identical to the conclusion.
The begging is rarely hidden in simple syllogisms.
3
Identify composite begging
In a composite syllogism, the conclusion reappears as a premise after a chain of reasoning. Example: 'Every human is a writer; every writer is a human; every human is laughing.' The major premise of the second syllogism is the original conclusion.
Composite begging is more concealed.
4
Note the figure condition
For universal affirmative conclusions, the author notes that begging can only occur in the first figure's first mood (Barbara). The begged premise is either the minor or major, while the other premise is true due to synonymy.
This applies specifically to conjunctive syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في المصادرة على المطلوب الأول. وكل مطلوب وقعت المصادرة به في الاحتجاج عليه، وإن كان داخلاً في جنس ما لم يبرهن، فليس ذلك مما ينعكس كنفسه؛ إذ ما لم يبرهن من المطلوبات قد يكون بأسباب أخرى غير المصادرة على المطلوب يأتي بيانها، بل المصادرة هو أن يؤخذ المطلوب المجهول نفسه مقدمة في بيان نفسه، إما بتبديل اسم أحد حديه بما يرادفه، وإما بغير تبديل، والواجب أن لا يُعرّف المجهول بنفسه، ولا بما هو أخفى منه، أو مثله، بل بما هو الأظهر، وذلك قد يقع في القياسات البسيطة، وقلما تخفي، وقد يقع في المركبة ويكون أكثر خفاء، وقد يكون ذلك في الاقترانات، وقد يكون الاستثنائيات، أما الاقترانات فمثال وقوعه في البسيط منها وهو ما أخذ فيه أحد حدّي المطلوب باسم ، ثم وضع حداً أوسط في القياس، وهو كما لو كان المطلوب مثلاً (كل إنسان ضاحك) فقلت (كل إنسان بشر) و(كل بشر ضاحك) فإنه وإن أنتج كل إنسان ضاحك فليس عين المطلوب غير المقدمة الكبرى، وأما مثال وقوعه في المركب منها، وهو ما بيّن فيه المطلوب بمقدمة غير بيّنة، وتلك بأخرى غير بينة بغير في المطلوب. وبالجملة فما المطلوب عينه مأخوذ في بيان ما بينه، أو ما يؤول في البيان إليه، وهو كما لو كان المطلوب كل إنسان ضاحك فقيل في بيانه كل إنسان كاتب وكل كاتب بشر وكل بشر ضاحك فإنه وإن كان يلزمه كل كاتب ضاحك فليس الكبرى من القياس الثاني غير ما كان نتيجة لنتيجته، وذلك قد يجري في الأشكال الأربعة، لكن إن كان المطلوب كلياً موجباً فما صودر فيه عليه كان بسيطاً أو مركباً، لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول، وإذ ذاك فببين جعله صغرى منه وكبرى لكبرى أو كان صغرى فهي مجهولة والكبرى صادقة من مترادفين، وإن كان كبرى فهي مجهولة والصغرى صادقة من مترادفين.
The first chapter on begging the question. Every sought conclusion in which begging occurs in the argument upon it, even if it falls under the genus of what is not demonstrated, that is not something that is reversed in itself; since what is not demonstrated from the sought conclusions may be due to other causes besides begging the question, the explanation of which will come. Rather, begging the question is that the unknown sought conclusion itself is taken as a premise in the proof of itself, either by changing the name of one of its two limits with a synonym, or without change. And it is obligatory that the unknown is not defined by itself, nor by what is more obscure than it, or similar to it, but by what is more apparent. And that may occur in simple syllogisms, and rarely are they hidden, and it may occur in composite ones and they are more hidden. And that may occur in conjunctive syllogisms and may occur in separative ones. As for conjunctive, an example of its occurrence in the simple kind—which is that one of the two limits of the sought conclusion is taken by name, then a middle term is placed in the syllogism—is as if the sought conclusion were, for example, 'Every human is laughing', and you said 'Every human is a human', 'Every human is laughing'. For even if it produces 'Every human is laughing', it is not the very sought conclusion except the major premise. As for an example of its occurrence in the composite kind—which is that the sought conclusion is proved by a non-evident premise, and that by another non-evident premise without [being] in the sought conclusion—is as if the sought conclusion were 'Every human is laughing', and in its proof it is said 'Every human is a writer', 'Every writer is a human', 'Every human is laughing'. For even if 'Every writer is laughing' follows from it, the major premise of the second syllogism is nothing but what was the result of its result. And that may occur in all four figures, but if the sought conclusion is affirmative universal, then what is begged in it, whether simple or composite, is not other than the first conjunctive of the first figure. And then it is clear to make it the minor premise of it and the major premise for the major, or if it is the minor, it is unknown and the major is true due to synonyms, and if it is the major, it is unknown and the minor is true due to synonyms.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage defines the logical fallacy known as begging the question (al-musadaratu 'ala al-matlub). The author begins by stating that every sought conclusion (matlub) that is begged in a proof, even if it falls under the genus of what is not yet demonstrated, is not something that is reversed in itself. This is because undemonstrated conclusions may remain unproven for other reasons, not solely due to begging the question. The proper definition is then given: begging the question occurs when the unknown conclusion itself is taken as a premise in its own proof, either by changing the name of one of its terms with a synonym or without any change. It is obligatory that the unknown be defined not by itself, nor by what is more obscure or equivalent, but by what is more apparent. This fallacy can occur in simple syllogisms (where it is rarely hidden) and in composite syllogisms (where it is more concealed). It can occur in both conjunctive (iqtirani) and separative (istithna'i) syllogisms. For conjunctive syllogisms, the author gives two examples: (1) Simple: If the sought conclusion is 'Every human is laughing', and one says 'Every human is a human' and 'Every human is laughing', the major premise is essentially the same as the conclusion, making the argument circular. (2) Composite: If the sought conclusion is 'Every human is laughing', and one says 'Every human is a writer', 'Every writer is a human', 'Every human is laughing', then the major premise of the second syllogism ('Every human is laughing') is the result of the previous result, again circular. The author notes that this fallacy can occur in all four figures of the syllogism, but if the sought conclusion is universal affirmative, the begging can only happen in the first figure's first conjunctive mood. In that case, the begged premise is either the minor or major premise, and the other premise is true due to synonymy. This passage is a typical expansion of the definition of a logical fallacy, illustrating it with concrete examples from categorical logic.

المطلوب
الحد

Key Takeaway
Begging the question is a fallacy where the conclusion is assumed as a premise, undermining the demonstration.

Examine the following argument from fiqh: 'Alcohol is haram because it is khamr, and khamr is haram.' Does it beg the question? Explain why or why not.
?

Classification of Begging the Question by Syllogistic Figures
Author classifies begging the question by figure and proposition type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which syllogistic figures allow begging the question for each type of categorical conclusion (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and explain the role of synonymy.

Core Idea
Begging the question is not random—it follows precise figure restrictions and uses synonymy for the other premise.

In this passage, the author does not just define begging the question—he gives a precise map of where it can and cannot occur in the four syllogistic figures. Understanding this map will help you detect circular arguments even when disguised by synonymy, a common trick in theological and legal debates. The rules are systematic: for each conclusion type, only certain figures are possible because the figure must be able to produce that conclusion in a valid syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
The classification depends on the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the sought conclusion (al-matlub). For example, universal affirmative, universal negative, etc.
Refer to the key terms: kulli mujib, kulli salib, juz'i mujib, juz'i salib.
2
Determine which figures can produce that conclusion
Recall the valid moods for each figure. For each conclusion type, only certain figures have valid moods. The author lists allowed figures: e.g., universal affirmative only from first figure; universal negative from first, second, or fourth, etc.
This step requires knowledge of syllogistic validity prior to this lesson.
3
Recognize the role of synonymy (mutaradifayn)
When begging the question occurs, the non-begged premise must be a synonym substitution of the other premise or the conclusion. The author says the other premise is 'true from synonyms' (sadiqa min mutaradifayn).
Synonymy hides the circularity; the same meaning is expressed with different words.
4
Apply the rules to a given syllogism
Given a syllogism aiming to prove a certain conclusion, check: (1) Is the figure one of the allowed ones for that conclusion type? (2) Does one premise appear to be the conclusion itself or a synonym? If so, it may be begging the question.
Use the example in the text: 'Every human is a writer, Every writer is a person, Every person is capable of laughter' as a compound case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فما المطلوب عينه مأخوذ في بيان ما بينه، أو ما يؤول في البيان إليه، وهو كما لو كان المطلوب كل إنسان (ضاحك فقيل في بيانه كل إنسان (كاتب و (كل كاتب بشر) و(كل بشر (ضاحك فإنه وإن كان يلزمه (كل) كاتب ضاحك فليس الكبرى من القياس الثاني غير ما كان نتيجة لنتيجته، وذلك قد يجري في الأشكال الأربعة، لكن إن كان المطلوب كلياً موجباً فما صودر عليه فيه كان بسيطاً أو مركباً، لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول، وإذ ذاك فببين جعله صغرى منه وكبرى لكبرى أو كان صغرى فهي مجهولة والكبرى صادقة من مترادفين، وإن كان كبرى فهي مجهولة والصغرى صادقة من مترادفين. وأما إن كان كلياً سالباً فما صودر عليه فيه إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، أو الرابع ، إذ الكلي لا يتم قياسه، أو قياس إحدى مقدمتيه من غير ما عددناه، فإن كان من الأول فلا يكون إلا كبرى والصغرى من مترادفين وإن كان من الثاني أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى، ويكون الحكم في الصغرى عند كونه كبرى وفي الكبرى عند كونه صغرى في الترادف ما سلف في الكلي الموجب، وإن كان من الرابع فلا يكون إلا صغرى والكبرى من مترادفين. وإن كان جزئياً موجباً فما صودر فيه عليه لا يكون من غير الأول والثالث والرابع، إذ الثاني لا ينتج غير السالب فلا يؤخذ في بيان الأول لم يمكن أن يكون إلا صغرى والكبرى من مترادفين، وإن كان الثالث من أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى، ويكون الحكم في ما اقترن به من المقدمة الأخرى في الترادف ما عرف وإن كان من الرابع لم يمكن أن يكون إلا كبرى والصغرى من مترادفين. وإن كان جزئياً سالباً فما صودر فيه عليه لا يكون من غير الثاني والثالث دون الأول والرابع إذ الجزئية السالبة فيهما لا تكون صغرى ولا كبرى، لكن إن كان من الثاني لم يكن منه إلا صغرى، وإن كان من الثالث لم يكن إلا كبرى، وحكم ما اقترن به في الترادف ما مضى.
In general, if the sought conclusion itself is taken in explaining something, or what ultimately leads to it in explanation, it is like if the sought conclusion is 'Every human is capable of laughter' and it is said in its proof 'Every human is a writer' and 'Every writer is a person' and 'Every person is capable of laughter'; for even if 'Every writer is capable of laughter' follows, the major premise of the second syllogism is not other than the result of its result. This can occur in the four figures. But if the sought conclusion is universal affirmative, then what is begged in it, whether simple or compound, cannot be other than the first conjunction of the first figure. In that case, it is made either a minor premise and a major premise for a major premise, or if it is a minor premise then it is unknown and the major premise is true from synonyms, and if it is a major premise then it is unknown and the minor premise is true from synonyms. If the sought conclusion is universal negative, then what is begged in it is either from the first figure, the second, or the fourth, since the universal negative syllogism is not completed except through one of these. If it is from the first, then it can only be a major premise and the minor premise is from synonyms. If it is from the second, it can be minor or major, and the ruling regarding the minor when it is major, and the major when it is minor, regarding synonymy, is as previously stated for the universal affirmative. If it is from the fourth, then it can only be a minor premise and the major premise is from synonyms. If the sought conclusion is particular affirmative, then what is begged in it can only be from the first, third, or fourth, since the second figure does not produce except negative; so it cannot be taken in explaining the affirmative. It can only be a minor premise and the major premise from synonyms. If it is from the third, it can be minor or major, and the ruling regarding what accompanies it from the other premise regarding synonymy is as known. If it is from the fourth, it can only be a major premise and the minor premise from synonyms. If the sought conclusion is particular negative, then what is begged in it can only be from the second or third, not the first or fourth, since the particular negative cannot be minor or major in them. But if it is from the second, it can only be a minor premise; if from the third, it can only be a major premise. The ruling regarding what accompanies it in synonymy is as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage expands on the general principle that begging the question can occur in any of the four syllogistic figures, but with specific constraints depending on the quantity and quality of the sought conclusion. The author first reiterates the definition with a compound example: a chain of syllogisms where the final major premise is the same as the conclusion of an earlier syllogism, thus circular. He then systematically lists the allowed figures for each case: (1) For a universal affirmative conclusion, begging occurs only in the first figure, and the begged premise can be minor or major, but the other accompanied premise must be from synonyms. (2) For a universal negative, figures 1, 2, and 4 are possible, again with synonym restrictions. (3) For a particular affirmative, figures 1, 3, and 4 (excluding the second figure, which yields only negatives). (4) For a particular negative, only figures 2 and 3. The pattern shows that the figure must be capable of producing the desired conclusion, and the non-begged premise must be a synonym substitution. This technical classification is meant for advanced students to detect subtle instances of the fallacy.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question follows a predictable pattern: for each conclusion type, only specific figures can host the fallacy, and the non-begged premise must be a synonym disguise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a syllogism from a kalam text: 'Every changing thing is contingent; the world is changing; therefore the world is contingent.' If the first premise is 'Everything contingent is changing' (the converse) and the second premise is 'The world is contingent' (the same as the conclusion), then it begs the question. Begging the Question in Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains begging the question in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conditional syllogism begs the question and distinguish the role of the conditional premise from the exceptional premise.

Core Idea
In conditional syllogisms, begging the question hides not in the conditional, but in the exception.

This lesson clarifies the subtle fallacy of begging the question in conditional syllogisms. The author of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq shows that in a simple connected conditional, the fallacy does not lie in the conditional premise itself, but in the exceptional premise (istithna'). Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to detect circular reasoning in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of a conditional syllogism
Identify the conditional premise (shartiyya) and the exceptional premise (istithna'iyya). The conditional premise is 'If A then B', and the exceptional premise affirms or denies A or B.
In a simple connected conditional, the antecedent and consequent must be the same in meaning for the fallacy to occur.
2
Check synonymy of antecedent and consequent
Examine if the antecedent and consequent are synonymous or identical in meaning. For example, 'If this is a human, then it is a person' — 'human' and 'person' are synonymous.
If they are not synonymous, the syllogism may still be valid without circularity.
3
Examine the exceptional premise
The author states that begging the question occurs through the exceptional premise, not the conditional. If you except the antecedent (e.g., 'this is a human'), the conclusion ('this is a person') follows but is already implicit.
The conditional premise alone does not entail the conclusion; it is the act of excepting that brings out the circularity.
4
Distinguish circular from valid arguments
Compare with a valid conditional syllogism where antecedent and consequent are not synonymous. For example, 'If this is a corpse, then it is najis' — the exception yields new knowledge.
The fallacy is not formal but material: the argument is formally valid but proves nothing new.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستثنائي فإما متصل وإما منفصل، والمتصل إما بسيط وإما مركب فإن كان بسيطاً فالمقدم لا بد وأن يكون هو عين التالي، إما بلفظين مترادفين أو ليس، والمصادرة فيه لا تكون إلا بالمقدمة الاستثنائية؛ إذ الشرطية ليس يلزم من تسليمها تسليم المطلوب، وذلك كما لو قيل إن كان هذا إنسان فهو بشر) فإنك إن استثنيت عين المقدم أنتج عين التالي فهو هو، وإن استثنيت نقيض التالي أنتج نقيض المقدم وهو هو. وإن كان مركباً فهو كما يقال في الهندسة إذا وقع خط مستقيم على خطين مستقيمين فصير الزاويتين اللتين من جهة واحدة معادلتين لقائمتين، فالخطان لا
As for the conditional syllogism, it is either connected or disjunctive. The connected is either simple or compound. If it is simple, then the antecedent must be the very same as the consequent, either with two synonymous phrases or not. In it, begging the question only occurs through the exceptional premise; for from the acceptance of the conditional premise, the acceptance of the sought conclusion does not necessarily follow. For example, if it is said 'If this is a human, then it is a person', then if you except the very antecedent, you produce the very consequent, and that is the sought conclusion itself; and if you except the contradictory of the consequent, you produce the contradictory of the antecedent, and that is also the sought conclusion itself. If it is compound, it is like what is said in geometry: 'If a straight line falls on two straight lines and makes the two interior angles on the same side equal to two right angles, then the two lines [are parallel]...'
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage addresses begging the question in conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author divides conditionals into connected (mutasil) and disjunctive (munfasil), and the connected further into simple and compound. For simple connected conditionals, the fallacy occurs when the antecedent is identical to the consequent in meaning, albeit possibly with synonym substitution. Importantly, the fallacy lies not in the conditional premise itself (shartiyya) but in the exceptional premise (muqaddima istithna'iyya): the conditional premise, even if true, does not entail the conclusion by itself; it is the act of excepting (istithna') the antecedent or the contradictory of the consequent that produces the conclusion. If the antecedent and consequent are synonymous, then excepting the antecedent yields the consequent, which is the same as the intended conclusion—thus begging the question. The author gives the example 'If this is a human, then it is a person': excepting the antecedent (this is a human) gives 'this is a person', which is the same as the consequent and thus obvious. Similarly, excepting the contradictory of the consequent gives the contradictory of the antecedent, again circular. For compound conditionals, he begins a geometric example about parallel lines, which is incomplete but illustrates a more complex chain.

Key Terms
الاستثنائي
متصل
منفصل
المقدم
التالي
المقدمة الاستثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) In the conclusion

Key Takeaway
In simple connected conditional syllogisms, begging the question arises solely from the exceptional premise when the antecedent and consequent are synonymous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'If this act is obligatory (wajib), then it is required (lazim).' Are the antecedent and consequent synonymous? Could this argument beg the question? Explain using the author's framework.

Detecting Begging the Question (Musadara) in Categorical Syllogisms
Author details begging the question conditions in categorical syllogisms by figure and proposition type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify musadara in any categorical syllogism by checking the figure and which premise is begged.

Core Idea
Musadara hides the conclusion in a premise through synonymity—its detection depends on the syllogism's figure and proposition type.

You already know the forms of valid categorical syllogisms. But what happens when an argument is circular? The logicians call this musadara (begging the question), often concealed by using synonymous terms. This passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq shows exactly how to detect musadara in any figure for three types of conclusions: universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Master this, and you will never be fooled by circular reasoning in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Determine whether the conclusion is universal negative (kulli salib), particular affirmative (juz'i mujib), or particular negative (juz'i salib). The author gives different rules for each.
The conclusion is the proposition that the syllogism aims to prove.
2
Recall which figures allow musadara for that type
For universal negative: figures 1, 2, 4. For particular affirmative: figures 1, 3, 4. For particular negative: figures 2, 3. If the syllogism's figure is not in the allowed set, musadara cannot occur in that form.
Figure 2 never yields a particular affirmative; figure 1 and 4 never yield a particular negative as a begged conclusion.
3
Locate the begged premise (major or minor)
For each allowed figure, the author specifies which premise must be the begged one. For example, in figure 1 with universal negative, the major is begged; in figure 2, either can be begged; in figure 4, the minor is begged. Use the passage to find the exact mapping.
The begged premise is the one that essentially states the conclusion itself.
4
Check the other premise for synonymity (taraduf)
The non-begged premise must be composed of two synonymous terms (like 'insan' and 'bashar') to conceal the circularity. If no synonymity is present, it may not be a case of musadara.
Synonymity is the key tactic; the argument appears to have two different premises but they mean the same thing.
5
Confirm the circularity
If the begged premise is indeed the conclusion in disguise, and the other premise is a tautology via synonymity, then the syllogism commits musadara. The argument proves nothing new.
This detection allows you to refute the argument in a debate (munazara).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان كلياً سالباً فما صودر عليه فيه إما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، أو الرابع ، إذ الكلي لا يتم قياسه، أو قياس إحدى مقدمتيه من غير ما عددناه، فإن كان من الأول فلا يكون إلا كبرى والصغرى من مترادفين وإن كان من الثاني أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى، ويكون الحكم في الصغرى عند كونه كبرى وفي الكبرى عند كونه صغرى في الترادف ما سلف في الكلي الموجب، وإن كان من الرابع فلا يكون إلا صغرى والكبرى من مترادفين.
وإن كان جزئياً موجباً فما صودر فيه عليه لا يكون من غير الأول والثالث والرابع، إذ الثاني لا ينتج غير السالب فلا يؤخذ في بيان الأول لم يمكن أن يكون إلا صغرى والكبرى من مترادفين، وإن كان الثالث من أمكن أن يكون صغرى وكبرى، ويكون الحكم في ما اقترن به من المقدمة الأخرى في الترادف ما عرف وإن كان من الرابع لم يمكن أن يكون إلا كبرى والصغرى من مترادفين.
وإن كان جزئياً سالباً فما صودر فيه عليه لا يكون من غير الثاني والثالث دون الأول والرابع إذ الجزئية السالبة فيهما لا تكون صغرى ولا كبرى، لكن إن كان من الثاني لم يكن منه إلا صغرى، وإن كان من الثالث لم يكن إلا كبرى، وحكم ما اقترن به في الترادف ما مضى.
As for if it is universal negative, then what is begged in it is either from the first figure or the second or the fourth, since the universal is not completed in its syllogism, or the syllogism of one of its premises from other than what we enumerated. So if it is from the first, it is only major, and the minor from two synonymous terms. And if it is from the second, it can be minor or major, and the ruling regarding the minor when it is major, and regarding the major when it is minor in synonymity is what passed in the universal affirmative. And if it is from the fourth, it is only minor, and the major from two synonymous terms.
And if it is particular affirmative, then what is begged in it is only from the first, third, and fourth, since the second does not produce except a negative, so it is not taken in clarifying the first. It cannot be except minor, and the major from two synonymous terms. And if it is from the third, it can be minor or major, and the ruling regarding what is conjoined with it from the other premise in synonymity is what is known. And if it is from the fourth, it cannot be except major, and the minor from two synonymous terms.
And if it is particular negative, then what is begged in it is only from the second and third, excluding the first and fourth, since the particular negative in them is neither minor nor major. But if it is from the second, it is only minor, and if it is from the third, it is only major, and the ruling regarding what is conjoined with it in synonymity has preceded.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage explains how begging the question (musadara) occurs in categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani), focusing on the figure (shakl) and the quantity/quality of the conclusion. The author considers three types of conclusions: universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For each, he specifies which figure(s) allow musadara and which premise (major or minor) must be the begged statement, noting that the other premise must involve synonymous terms (taraduf) to conceal the circularity.

For universal negative conclusions: Musadara can occur in figures 1, 2, or 4. In figure 1, the begged premise is the major, and the minor is from synonymous terms. In figure 2, either premise can be begged, with the other being synonymous. In figure 4, the begged premise is the minor.

For particular affirmative conclusions: Only figures 1, 3, and 4 are possible (figure 2 yields only negatives). In figure 1, the minor is begged, major synonymous. In figure 3, either premise can be begged. In figure 4, the major is begged.

For particular negative conclusions: Only figures 2 and 3 are possible. In figure 2, the minor is begged; in figure 3, the major is begged.

The recurring principle is that the non-begged premise must be composed of synonymous terms (like 'insan' and 'bashar') so that the argument appears novel but is actually circular. This analysis is critical for detecting fallacious reasoning in Islamic logical debates.

Key Terms
مصادرة (musadara): begging the question; taking the conclusion itself as a premise in the syllogism, often concealed by synonymity.
شكل (shakl): figure of the categorical syllogism; arrangement of the middle term in the premises. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
ترادف (taraduf): synonymity; using two synonymous terms (e.g., 'insan' and 'bashar') as middle term to hide the circularity.
كلي سالب (kulli salib): universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B').
جزئي موجب (juz'i mujib): particular affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Some A is B').
جزئي سالب (juz'i salib): particular negative proposition (e.g., 'Some A is not B').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which figure(s) can musadara occur for a particular affirmative conclusion?
A) Figure 1 only
B) Figures 1 and 2
C) Figures 1, 3, and 4
D) Figures 2 and 3

Key Takeaway
Musadara is detected by matching the conclusion type to the allowed figures and identifying which premise is begged, with the other premise using synonymity to hide the circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'No intoxicant is permissible (halal). Khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore khamr is not permissible.' The conclusion is universal negative. In which figure(s) could musadara occur? Identify the begged premise if the syllogism is in figure 1.
?
A scholar argues: 'Some acts are obligatory (wajib) because they are commanded in the Qur'an. The act of prayer is commanded in the Qur'an. Therefore prayer is obligatory.' The conclusion is particular affirmative. In which figure(s) is musadara possible? Which premise would be begged if the figure is 3?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Musadara in Simple Conjunctive Conditional Syllogisms
Author examines begging the question in conjunctive and disjunctive conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect begging the question (musadara) in a simple conjunctive conditional syllogism and identify that it can only occur in the exceptive premise.

Core Idea
In simple conjunctive conditionals, the fallacy hides in the exceptive premise, not the conditional.

Conditional syllogisms are powerful reasoning tools, but they can be misused when the conclusion is assumed in a premise. Classical logicians precisely analyzed how musadara occurs. In this lesson, we focus on the simple conjunctive case, pinpointing where the fallacy lies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
A simple conjunctive conditional syllogism has a major premise of the form 'if A then B' and a minor premise that either affirms A (modus ponens) or denies B (modus tollens). Write out both valid forms.
2
Locate where musadara can occur
The author states that in these syllogisms, musadara can only be in the exceptive premise, not the conditional. Reason: granting the conditional alone does not yield the conclusion; the fallacy requires that the minor premise itself be the conclusion in disguise.
The conditional may be true and yet the argument still beg the question if the minor premise assumes the conclusion.
3
Test an example from the source
Consider: 'If this is a human, then it is a mortal.' The author says if you except the exact antecedent ('this is a human'), you produce the exact consequent ('it is a mortal'). Musadara would occur if the exceptive premise is itself the very claim to be proven — i.e., if it is synonymous with the conclusion.
The passage notes that the antecedent and consequent must be 'exactly the same' in meaning for the fallacy to arise in this manner.
4
Apply an Islamic example
Example: 'If something is khamr (wine), then it is haram. This is khamr. Therefore it is haram.' This is valid, not fallacious. A fallacious version would be: 'If something is khamr, then it is haram. This is haram. Therefore it is khamr?' That is affirming the consequent, not musadara. True musadara occurs if the minor premise is 'this is haram' and the conclusion is also 'this is haram', i.e., the minor premise is the conclusion itself.
Ensure you distinguish musadara from other fallacies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستثنائي فإما متصل وإما منفصل، والمتصل إما بسيط وإما مركب فإن كان بسيطاً فالمقدم لا بد وأن يكون هو عين التالي، إما بلفظين مترادفين أو ليس، والمصادرة فيه لا تكون إلا بالمقدمة الاستثنائية؛ إذ الشرطية ليس يلزم من تسليمها تسليم المطلوب، وذلك كما لو قيل إن كان هذا إنسان فهو بشر) فإنك إن استثنيت عين المقدم أنتج عين التالي فهو هو، وإن استثنيت نقيض التالي أنتج نقيض المقدم وهو هو.
وإن كان مركباً فهو كما يقال في الهندسة إذا وقع خط مستقيم على خطين مستقيمين فصير الزاويتين اللتين من جهة واحدة معادلتين لقائمتين، فالخطان لا يلتقيان، وإلا فلو التقيا في جهة لحدث منهما مع الواقع عليهما مثلث زواياه أعظم من قائمتين؛ لكون الزاويتين اللتين عند قاعدته مساوية لقائمتين والحادثة من الخطين زائدة عليهما وهو خلف؛ إذ زوايا المثلث مساوية لقائمتين، إنما تبرهن بعد صحة ذلك الوضع، فيكون قد بيّن حال الزوايا في الخطين بما لا يتم بيانه إلا به، وهو مصادرة على المطلوب.
وأما إن كان منفصلاً فإما بسيط وإما مركب فإن كان بسيطاً لم يتصور فيه مصادرة على المطلوب، وإلا فالمصادرة إما في المنفصلة أو الاستثنائية منه، لا جائز أن يكون في المنفصلة؛ إذ التسليم لها لا يكون تسليماً للمطلوب، ولا في الاستثنائية؛ إذ هي أبداً في المنفصلة تكون مقابلة للمطلوب، والمقابل للمطلوب كيف يصادر فيه على عين المطلوب؟ وأما إن كان مركباً فعلى هذا النحو أيضاً، وربما أخذ في القياس شيئان متعاكسان كـ الإنسان والناطق) أو جزئي وكلي كـ (الإنسان والحيوان فيظن أن حكمهما واحد ثم يبنى عليه ظن المصادرة على المطلوب الأول، وفي التحقيق ليس ذلك مصادرة إلا بحسب الظن، وهو على أقسام نذكرها في طوبيقا، وعند تحقيق ما أشرنا إليه من حقيقي المصادرة على المطلوب وظنية فما صودر فيه على المطلوب قد يشابه القياس الكائن من المقدمات المتقابلة في أخذ الحد الأوسط في النتيجة، وإن افترقا في لا بُدّية كذب إحدى المقدمتين المتقابلتين في القياس المؤلف منهما، وعدم هذه اللا بُدّية في المصادرة؛ إذ ليس يمتنع أن يكون المطلوب ـع ما اقترن به حقاً، وفي أن المصادرة يُبيّن به مطلوب غير بين، وفي القياس المتقابل نقيض مطلوب مسلّم.
As for the exceptive [syllogism], it is either conjunctive or disjunctive. The conjunctive is either simple or compound. If it is simple, then the antecedent must be exactly the consequent, either by two synonymous terms or [not]. And begging the question in it is only by the exceptive premise, since from granting the conditional does not necessarily follow granting the desired. For example, if it is said, 'If this is a human, then he is a mortal,' if you except exactly the antecedent, it produces exactly the consequent, so it is the same, and if you except the contradictory of the consequent, it produces the contradictory of the antecedent, and it is the same.
If it is compound, it is like what is said in geometry: if a straight line falls upon two straight lines making the two angles on one side equal to two right angles, then the two lines do not meet. Otherwise, if they met on one side, a triangle would be formed with angles greater than two right angles, because the two angles at its base equal two right angles, and the [angle] formed by the two lines exceeds them, which is a contradiction, since the angles of a triangle are equal to two right angles. This is only proven after the correctness of that position, so he has explained the condition of the angles in the two lines by what cannot be explained except by it, and that is begging the question.
As for if it is disjunctive, it is either simple or compound. If it is simple, begging the question regarding the desired cannot be conceived in it. Otherwise, begging the question would be either in the disjunctive or the exceptive. It is not possible that it be in the disjunctive, because granting it does not grant the desired; nor in the exceptive, because it is always in the disjunctive opposite to the desired. How could the opposite of the desired be begged as the very desired? And if it is compound, it is on this same pattern. And perhaps two mutually reversed things are taken in the syllogism, like 'human' and 'rational', or a particular and a universal like 'human' and 'animal', so one thinks their ruling is one, then builds on it the suspicion of begging the first desired. In reality, that is not begging except by suspicion. It has divisions that we will mention in Topics. And upon verifying what we have pointed out regarding real and suspected begging the question: what is begged in the desired may resemble the syllogism composed of contradictory premises in taking the middle term in the conclusion, although they differ in the necessity of the falsehood of one of the two contradictory premises in the syllogism composed of them, and the absence of this necessity in begging the question, since it is not impossible that the desired along with what is conjoined with it be true. And in that, begging the question explains a non-evident desired, while in the contradictory syllogism it explains the contradictory of a granted desired.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage extends the analysis of begging the question (musadara) to conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author first divides them into conjunctive (mutasil) and disjunctive (munfasil), each being simple or compound.

For simple conjunctive conditional syllogisms: The antecedent must be identical in meaning to the consequent (e.g., 'If it is a human, it is a mortal'—where 'human' and 'mortal' are not identical, so the author notes 'or not'? Actually the text says the antecedent must be exactly the consequent, either by two synonymous terms or not. This is ambiguous. The example given shows that if you state 'If this is a human, then he is a mortal', the terms are not synonymous; here musadara would occur if the exceptive premise ('This is a human') is actually assumed from the conclusion. The author says musadara in simple conjunctive can only occur through the exceptive premise, not the conditional premise, because granting the conditional alone does not yield the conclusion. The exceptive premise must be the conclusion itself (or a synonymous form).

For compound conjunctive: The author gives a geometric example of proving that two lines do not meet based on a property of angles that itself relies on the very theorem being proved. This is a classic case of circular reasoning.

For simple disjunctive: Musadara is impossible because the disjunctive premise does not assert the conclusion, and the exceptive premise is always the opposite of the desired conclusion. So you cannot beg the question.

For compound disjunctive: Same reasoning applies.

The author then discusses apparent musadara when mutually reversed terms (like 'human' and 'rational') or partial/universal terms (like 'human' and 'animal') are used, which may create a suspicion of circularity but is not true musadara. He distinguishes true musadara from syllogisms with contradictory premises: in musadara, the premises can be true, but the argument does not prove a new truth; in contradictory premises, one premise must be false. This clarifies the nature of the fallacy.

مقدم
تالي

Key Takeaway
In simple conjunctive conditional syllogisms, musadara can only occur through the exceptive premise, not the conditional.

Musadara in Compound Conjunctive Conditional Syllogisms
Author examines begging the question in conjunctive and disjunctive conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular reasoning in a compound conjunctive conditional syllogism using the geometric example provided by the author.

Core Idea
Compound conjunctive conditionals can hide circularity when a key step depends on the very claim being proved.

Not all conditional syllogisms are simple; some involve multiple conditionals chained together. The author presents a geometric example to show how a proof can become circular when a premise relies on the conclusion. This lesson walks through that example and draws lessons for constructing sound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the compound conjunctive structure
A compound conjunctive conditional syllogism involves a chain of conditionals where the consequent of one becomes the antecedent of another, leading to a final conclusion. The geometric example uses such a chain.
The example: if a straight line falls on two lines making interior angles equal to two right angles, then the lines do not meet. If they met, a triangle would have angle sum > 180°, which is impossible (contradiction).
2
Identify the circularity
The author argues that the proof that the triangle's angles sum to 180° depends on the very theorem being proved (the parallel postulate). Thus, the argument uses the conclusion to prove itself.
This is a classic case of musadara: the premise (angles sum to 180°) is not independently established but is assumed in the demonstration.
3
Generalize the pattern
In a compound conditional, the fallacy occurs when an intermediate conclusion is itself the final claim. The argument appears to progress but actually reverts to the starting point.
Always check if any step in the chain presupposes the truth of the overall conclusion.
4
Apply to Islamic reasoning
For instance, in proving that the Qur'an is the word of Allah by its inimitability (i'jaz), one might argue: 'If the Qur'an is inimitable, then it is from Allah. The Qur'an is from Allah (because it is inimitable?)...' This would be circular if the inimitability is based on its divine origin.
Ensure that premises are independent of the conclusion to avoid musadara.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستثنائي فإما متصل وإما منفصل، والمتصل إما بسيط وإما مركب فإن كان بسيطاً فالمقدم لا بد وأن يكون هو عين التالي، إما بلفظين مترادفين أو ليس، والمصادرة فيه لا تكون إلا بالمقدمة الاستثنائية؛ إذ الشرطية ليس يلزم من تسليمها تسليم المطلوب، وذلك كما لو قيل إن كان هذا إنسان فهو بشر) فإنك إن استثنيت عين المقدم أنتج عين التالي فهو هو، وإن استثنيت نقيض التالي أنتج نقيض المقدم وهو هو.
وإن كان مركباً فهو كما يقال في الهندسة إذا وقع خط مستقيم على خطين مستقيمين فصير الزاويتين اللتين من جهة واحدة معادلتين لقائمتين، فالخطان لا يلتقيان، وإلا فلو التقيا في جهة لحدث منهما مع الواقع عليهما مثلث زواياه أعظم من قائمتين؛ لكون الزاويتين اللتين عند قاعدته مساوية لقائمتين والحادثة من الخطين زائدة عليهما وهو خلف؛ إذ زوايا المثلث مساوية لقائمتين، إنما تبرهن بعد صحة ذلك الوضع، فيكون قد بيّن حال الزوايا في الخطين بما لا يتم بيانه إلا به، وهو مصادرة على المطلوب.

As for the exceptive [syllogism], it is either conjunctive or disjunctive. The conjunctive is either simple or compound. If it is simple, then the antecedent must be exactly the consequent, either by two synonymous terms or [not]. And begging the question in it is only by the exceptive premise, since from granting the conditional does not necessarily follow granting the desired. For example, if it is said, 'If this is a human, then he is a mortal,' if you except exactly the antecedent, it produces exactly the consequent, so it is the same, and if you except the contradictory of the consequent, it produces the contradictory of the antecedent, and it is the same.
If it is compound, it is like what is said in geometry: if a straight line falls upon two straight lines making the two angles on one side equal to two right angles, then the two lines do not meet. Otherwise, if they met on one side, a triangle would be formed with angles greater than two right angles, because the two angles at its base equal two right angles, and the [angle] formed by the two lines exceeds them, which is a contradiction, since the angles of a triangle are equal to two right angles. This is only proven after the correctness of that position, so he has explained the condition of the angles in the two lines by what cannot be explained except by it, and that is begging the question.
As for if it is disjunctive, it is either simple or compound. If it is simple, begging the question regarding the desired cannot be conceived in it. Otherwise, begging the question would be either in the disjunctive or the exceptive. It is not possible that it be in the disjunctive, because granting it does not grant the desired; nor in the exceptive, because it is always in the disjunctive opposite to the desired. How could the opposite of the desired be begged as the very desired? And if it is compound, it is on this same pattern. And perhaps two mutually reversed things are taken in the syllogism, like 'human' and 'rational', or a particular and a universal like 'human' and 'animal', so one thinks their ruling is one, then builds on it the suspicion of begging the first desired. In reality, that is not begging except by suspicion. It has divisions that we will mention in Topics. And in that, begging the question explains a non-evident desired, while in the contradictory syllogism it explains the contradictory of a granted desired.

Commentary
This passage extends the analysis of begging the question (musadara) to conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author first divides them into conjunctive (mutasil) and disjunctive (munfasil), each being simple or compound.

For simple conjunctive conditional syllogisms: The antecedent must be identical in meaning to the consequent (e.g., 'If it is a human, it is a mortal'—where 'human' and 'mortal' are not identical, so the author notes 'or not'? Actually the text says the antecedent must be exactly the consequent, either by two synonymous terms or not. This is ambiguous. The example given shows that if you state 'If this is a human, then he is a mortal', the terms are not synonymous; here musadara would occur if the exceptive premise ('This is a human') is actually assumed from the conclusion. The author says musadara in simple conjunctive can only occur through the exceptive premise, not the conditional premise, because granting the conditional alone does not yield the conclusion. The exceptive premise must be the conclusion itself (or a synonymous form).

For compound conjunctive: The author gives a geometric example of proving that two lines do not meet based on a property of angles that itself relies on the very theorem being proved. This is a classic case of circular reasoning.

For simple disjunctive: Musadara is impossible because the disjunctive premise does not assert the conclusion, and the exceptive premise is always the opposite of the desired conclusion. So you cannot beg the question.

For compound disjunctive: Same reasoning applies.

The author then discusses apparent musadara when mutually reversed terms (like 'human' and 'rational') or partial/universal terms (like 'human' and 'animal') are used, which may create a suspicion of circularity but is not true musadara. He distinguishes true musadara from syllogisms with contradictory premises: in musadara, the premises can be true, but the argument does not prove a new truth; in contradictory premises, one premise must be false. This clarifies the nature of the fallacy.

In compound conjunctive conditionals, musadara occurs when an intermediate step implicitly relies on the final conclusion, creating a circular chain.

The Syllogism

Why Musadara Cannot Occur in Simple Disjunctive Conditional Syllogisms
Author examines begging the question in conjunctive and disjunctive conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why begging the question is impossible in a simple disjunctive conditional syllogism, based on the logical structure.

Core Idea
In a simple disjunctive conditional, the exceptive premise is always opposite to the desired conclusion, so it cannot assume it.

The author extends the analysis to disjunctive conditionals and makes a striking claim: musadara is impossible in a simple disjunctive syllogism. This lesson unpacks the logical reason: the exceptive premise (which affirms or denies one disjunct) necessarily contradicts the desired conclusion if it is to be the conclusion. Therefore, the fallacy cannot arise.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of a disjunctive conditional syllogism
A disjunctive major premise is of the form 'either A or B' (exclusive or inclusive). The minor premise either affirms one disjunct or denies one, leading to a conclusion about the other. Write the valid forms.
2
Consider what musadara would require
For musadara to occur, the exceptive premise would have to be the very conclusion we seek to prove. But in a disjunctive, the conclusion is always about the disjunct not mentioned in the exceptive premise.
If the exceptive premise is 'A', the conclusion is 'not B' (or 'B' depending on exclusivity). The exceptive premise is thus the opposite of a possible conclusion that concerns B.
3
Understand the author's argument
The author says: musadara would have to be in either the disjunctive premise or the exceptive premise. The disjunctive premise cannot contain it because granting it does not grant the desired. The exceptive premise cannot contain it because it is always 'muqabil' (opposite) to the desired. Thus, it is impossible.
This reasoning shows that the structure itself prevents the fallacy.
4
Apply to Islamic reasoning
Example: 'Either this act is wajib or it is mubah. It is not wajib. Therefore it is mubah.' The exceptive premise ('not wajib') is the opposite of the conclusion if we wanted to prove 'it is wajib'? But here the conclusion is different. If the desired conclusion were 'it is wajib', the exceptive premise would be 'not wajib' which is its opposite, so no circularity.
Musadara cannot sneak in because the exceptive premise always negates part of the desired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستثنائي فإما متصل وإما منفصل، والمتصل إما بسيط وإما مركب فإن كان بسيطاً فالمقدم لا بد وأن يكون هو عين التالي، إما بلفظين مترادفين أو ليس، والمصادرة فيه لا تكون إلا بالمقدمة الاستثنائية؛ إذ الشرطية ليس يلزم من تسليمها تسليم المطلوب، وذلك كما لو قيل إن كان هذا إنسان فهو بشر) فإنك إن استثنيت عين المقدم أنتج عين التالي فهو هو، وإن استثنيت نقيض التالي أنتج نقيض المقدم وهو هو.
وإن كان مركباً فهو كما يقال في الهندسة إذا وقع خط مستقيم على خطين مستقيمين فصير الزاويتين اللتين من جهة واحدة معادلتين لقائمتين، فالخطان لا يلتقيان، وإلا فلو التقيا في جهة لحدث منهما مع الواقع عليهما مثلث زواياه أعظم من قائمتين؛ لكون الزاويتين اللتين عند قاعدته مساوية لقائمتين والحادثة من الخطين زائدة عليهما وهو خلف؛ إذ زوايا المثلث مساوية لقائمتين، إنما تبرهن بعد صحة ذلك الوضع، فيكون قد بيّن حال الزوايا في الخطين بما لا يتم بيانه إلا به، وهو مصادرة على المطلوب.

As for the exceptive [syllogism], it is either conjunctive or disjunctive. The conjunctive is either simple or compound. If it is simple, then the antecedent must be exactly the consequent, either by two synonymous terms or [not]. And begging the question in it is only by the exceptive premise, since from granting the conditional does not necessarily follow granting the desired. For example, if it is said, 'If this is a human, then he is a mortal,' if you except exactly the antecedent, it produces exactly the consequent, so it is the same, and if you except the contradictory of the consequent, it produces the contradictory of the antecedent, and it is the same.
If it is compound, it is like what is said in geometry: if a straight line falls upon two straight lines making the two angles on one side equal to two right angles, then the two lines do not meet. Otherwise, if they met on one side, a triangle would be formed with angles greater than two right angles, because the two angles at its base equal two right angles, and the [angle] formed by the two lines exceeds them, which is a contradiction, since the angles of a triangle are equal to two right angles. This is only proven after the correctness of that position, so he has explained the condition of the angles in the two lines by what cannot be explained except by it, and that is begging the question.
As for if it is disjunctive, it is either simple or compound. If it is simple, begging the question regarding the desired cannot be conceived in it. Otherwise, begging the question would be either in the disjunctive or the exceptive. It is not possible that it be in the disjunctive, because granting it does not grant the desired; nor in the exceptive, because it is always in the disjunctive opposite to the desired. How could the opposite of the desired be begged as the very desired? And if it is compound, it is on this same pattern. And perhaps two mutually reversed things are taken in the syllogism, like 'human' and 'rational', or a particular and a universal like 'human' and 'animal', so one thinks their ruling is one, then builds on it the suspicion of begging the first desired. In reality, that is not begging except by suspicion. It has divisions that we will mention in Topics. And upon verifying what we have pointed out regarding real and suspected begging the question: what is begged in the desired may resemble the syllogism composed of contradictory premises in taking the middle term in the conclusion, although they differ in the necessity of the falsehood of one of the two contradictory premises in the syllogism composed of them, and the absence of this necessity in begging the question, since it is not impossible that the desired along with what is conjoined with it be true. And in that, begging the question explains a non-evident desired, while in the contradictory syllogism it explains the contradictory of a granted desired.

Commentary
This passage extends the analysis of begging the question (musadara) to conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author first divides them into conjunctive (mutasil) and disjunctive (munfasil), each being simple or compound.

For simple conjunctive conditional syllogisms: The antecedent must be identical in meaning to the consequent (e.g., 'If it is a human, it is a mortal'—where 'human' and 'mortal' are not identical, so the author notes 'or not'? Actually the text says the antecedent must be exactly the consequent, either by two synonymous terms or not. This is ambiguous. The example given shows that if you state 'If this is a human, then he is a mortal', the terms are not synonymous; here musadara would occur if the exceptive premise ('This is a human') is actually assumed from the conclusion. The author says musadara in simple conjunctive can only occur through the exceptive premise, not the conditional premise, because granting the conditional alone does not yield the conclusion. The exceptive premise must be the conclusion itself (or a synonymous form).

For compound conjunctive: The author gives a geometric example of proving that two lines do not meet based on a property of angles that itself relies on the very theorem being proved. This is a classic case of circular reasoning.

For simple disjunctive: Musadara is impossible because the disjunctive premise does not assert the conclusion, and the exceptive premise is always the opposite of the desired conclusion. So you cannot beg the question.

For compound disjunctive: Same reasoning applies.

The author then discusses apparent musadara when mutually reversed terms (like 'human' and 'rational') or partial/universal terms (like 'human' and 'animal') are used, which may create a suspicion of circularity but is not true musadara. He distinguishes true musadara from syllogisms with contradictory premises: in musadara, the premises can be true, but the argument does not prove a new truth; in contradictory premises, one premise must be false. This clarifies the nature of the fallacy.

True Conclusions from False Premises: The 'Alone' Objection Resolved
Author explains how true conclusions can follow from false premises and resolves an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the 'alone' confusion in syllogisms and explain why true premises are not necessary for a true conclusion.

Core Idea
False premises can yield a true conclusion; 'alone' is not part of the subject.

This lesson tackles a crucial fallacy: assuming that a true conclusion guarantees true premises. The author demonstrates that false premises can yield true conclusions, and clarifies a common confusion with the word 'alone' (wahdahu), which is often mistakenly treated as part of the subject term.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle of non-reversibility
Understand that truth flows from premises to conclusion, but not vice versa. False premises can still yield a true conclusion.
This is a key meta-logical point.
2
Examine the objection
Consider the example: 'Man alone is rational; every rational is animal; therefore man alone is animal.' Why does this seem to contradict the principle?
The premises appear true, but the conclusion seems false.
3
Identify the ambiguous premise
The minor premise 'Man alone is rational' is unquantified (muhmala) and includes the extraneous word 'alone', which creates confusion about the subject.
The premise could be interpreted universally or particularly, leading to falsity.
4
Strip the extraneous term
Recognize that 'alone' functions like a quantifier or modality, not part of the subject. Remove it to get the correct minor term: 'Man is rational'.
The stripped syllogism becomes: Man is rational; every rational is animal; therefore man is animal.
5
Test the corrected syllogism
The corrected syllogism is valid (first figure, Barbara) with a true conclusion. The addition of 'alone' is irrelevant to the logical relation.
This demonstrates that the original objection rested on a misanalysis.

Chapter Four: On how to derive truth from falsehood. We say: Even though the entailment of a true conclusion from true premises is the entailment of the consequent from the antecedent, it does not follow from the removal of the antecedent the removal of its consequent, nor from positing the same consequent the same antecedent, as previously stated. So it does not follow from the removal of the truth of the premises the removal of the truth of the conclusion, nor from the removal of the truth of the conclusion the truth of the premises; rather, it is possible that the premises are false and the conclusion is true, and there is no difference in that between a categorical or conditional [syllogism]. And for the non-trained it is necessary to clarify that with examples, even though sound natural disposition suffices in that. And before delving into that, it is necessary to allude to solving a problem in which much confusion has occurred, raised against the statement that true premises entail a true conclusion, which is our statement: 'Man alone is rational, and every rational is an animal'—two true premises, and the necessary conclusion from them is 'Man alone is an animal', which is false. And it was said in its solution that the falsity of the conclusion was only due to the falsity of the minor premise, namely that the speaker's saying 'Man alone is rational' is an unquantified proposition [qadiyya muhmala], and it is either meant as 'every man' or 'some men'. If meant universally, its meaning is 'every single man alone is rational' as previously, and it is not hidden that the statement that every single man alone is rational is false; because from the fact that every single man alone is rational it follows that no one other than him among men is rational. And if meant particularly, it is not hidden that the judgment about some men that they alone are rational is also false. And [this] although it is true, yet it is not uniform, as in our statement: 'Zayd alone is in this place, and everyone found in this place is a body'—for it entails 'Zayd alone is a body', which is false, despite the truth of the two premises. And the correct approach in solving it is to say: The confusion here only arose from the assumption that 'alone' is part of the subject in the minor premise, but it is not so. For there may be mentioned in the premises of the syllogism what is not part of the subject nor of the predicate, such as the quantifier [sur] and modality [jiha] and the like. So our saying 'Man alone is rational' indicates affirming rationality of man and negating it of non-man, and its equivalent is our saying 'Man is rational and non-man is not rational'. The subject for the affirmation of rationality is only man, considered abstracted from 'alone'. Therefore our saying 'Man is rational' is true when abstracted from 'alone'. So at the time of production, the minor and major terms must be stripped of everything that has no role in them. The minor term is 'man', and the major is 'body'. It is not hidden that the relation of 'body' to 'man' is true and not false. And what 'alone' indicates—namely the negation of rationality from non-man—has no role in this syllogism; rather it was introduced into it as if foreign. And at this point, we return to where we stopped, and we say: Every categorical [syllogism]—its two premises are either both universal, or one of them is particular. If both are universal, the falsehood is either in both of them or in one of them. If it is in both, then either in each one of them or in part of them, or in each one and part of the other. And if the falsehood is in each one of them, then an example from the first figure—both being affirmative—is that in which the true [conclusion] comes from the relation of the middle term to the two extremes by universal negation, such as our saying: 'Every man is an inanimate and every inanimate is an animal'. And an example from it [first figure] with the major premise negative is that in which the true [conclusion] comes from the relation of the middle term to the minor by universal negation and the relation of the major to the middle by universal affirmation, such as our saying: 'Every inanimate is a man and no man is an animal' so 'No inanimate is an animal'. And if it is from the second figure, then an example—with the major premise negative—is that in which the true [conclusion] comes from the relation of the middle term to the minor by negation and to the major by universal affirmation, such as our saying: 'Every horse is a man and no laugher is a man' so 'No horse is a laugher'. And an example—with the minor premise negative—is that in which the true [conclusion] comes from the relation of the middle term to the two extremes in the opposite of the first, such as our saying: 'Nothing of man is rational and every horse is rational' so 'Nothing of man is a horse'. And if it is from the third figure, then an example—both premises affirmative—is that in which the true [conclusion] comes from the relation
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage explains a key logical principle: true conclusions can follow from false premises. The author begins by stating that just as a true conclusion necessarily follows from true premises (like a consequent from an antecedent), the converse is not required—removing truth of premises does not remove truth of conclusion, nor does assuming a true conclusion imply true premises. This holds for both categorical and conditional syllogisms. The author then addresses a common objection: the example 'Man alone is rational; every rational is an animal; therefore man alone is an animal.' The premises are thought true but the conclusion false, which seems to contradict the principle. The solution is that the minor premise 'Man alone is rational' is ambiguous. If interpreted universally, it means every individual man alone is rational, which is false because it implies no other man is rational. If particularly, it is also false as a universal claim about some men. The real confusion arises from treating 'alone' as part of the subject, whereas it is like a quantifier or modality—it indicates affirmation of rationality to man and negation to non-man. In the syllogism, the minor term is simply 'man' (stripped of 'alone'), and the major is 'body'. The relation 'man is body' is true, so the conclusion 'man is body' (not 'man alone is body') is true. The 'alone' part is irrelevant to the syllogism's validity. The author then returns to the main discussion and categorizes false premises in categorical syllogisms: both universal, or one particular. For both universal, falsehood can be in both or one, with subdivisions. Examples are given from the first, second, and third figures showing how false premises can produce a true conclusion. For instance, first figure both affirmative: 'Every man is inanimate; every inanimate is an animal' yields 'Every man is animal' (true). First figure with negative major: 'Every inanimate is man; no man is animal' yields 'No inanimate is animal' (true). Second figure examples and third figure examples follow. The passage uses Islamic science examples (man, animal, inanimate, horse, laugher) to illustrate logical forms.

Key Terms
اقتران (iqtiran) – categorical syllogism
استثنائي (istithna'i) – conditional syllogism
وحده (wahdahu) – 'alone'; extraneous modifier
مهملة (muhmala) – unquantified proposition
السور (al-sur) – quantifier
الجهة (al-jiha) – modality

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the conclusion 'Man alone is an animal' false in the example 'Man alone is rational; every rational is an animal'?
A) Because the minor premise is false
B) Because the major premise is false
C) Because 'alone' is incorrectly treated as part of the subject
D) Because the syllogism is invalid

Key Takeaway
True premises are sufficient but not necessary for a true conclusion; extraneous modifiers like 'alone' must be stripped from syllogistic terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyse the following argument: 'Every rational being is accountable; every human is rational; therefore every human is accountable.' Now change the minor premise to 'Every human alone is rational.' Does the conclusion still follow? Explain the difference using the lesson's principle.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Purifying the Terms: Removing Extraneous Elements
Author explains why qualifiers like 'alone' must be removed from the minor and major terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and remove quantifiers and modalities that are not part of the logical subject or predicate in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Only the bare subject and predicate matter; all else must be stripped for the syllogism to work.

When we construct a categorical syllogism, the minor and major terms must be presented in their pure form, without any extraneous words like 'alone' or modal expressions. The author gives the example of 'man alone is rational' – the word 'alone' implies a negation from non-man, but that nuance is irrelevant to the logical relation between the terms. In this lesson, you will learn to identify such extraneous elements and strip them away, leaving only the essential terms required for syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the extraneous element
Look for words that are not part of the subject or predicate themselves, such as quantifiers (e.g., 'every', 'some'), modalities (e.g., 'necessarily', 'possibly'), or exclusivity markers (e.g., 'alone', 'only'). In the phrase 'man alone is rational', the word 'alone' is extraneous.
2
Determine what the extraneous element adds
In 'man alone is rational', 'alone' adds the implication that non-man is not rational. This is a separate proposition, not part of the basic affirmation that man is rational.
3
Formulate the stripped version
Write the proposition with only the subject and predicate and the copula: 'Man is rational.' This is the form used in syllogisms.
4
Apply to a syllogism example
In the given syllogism, the minor term is 'man' and the major is 'body'. The relation 'body is man' is true, but the added 'alone' has no function. Ensure both premises are stripped before checking validity.

والموضوع لإيجاب الناطق له إنما الإنسان مقطوعاً فيه النظر عن وحده؛ ولهذا يصدق قولنا: الإنسان ناطق مجرداً عن وحده، فعند الإنتاج وجب أن يجرد الحد الأصغر والأكبر عن كل ما لا مدخل له فيهما، والحد الأصغر هو الإنسان، والأكبر هو الجسم، ولا يخفى أن نسبة الجسم إلى الإنسان صادق وليس بكاذب، وما دلت وحده من سلب الناطق عن غير الإنسان، فلا مدخل له في هذا القياس. وعند هذا فنعود إلى حيث وقفنا فنقول كل اقتران فمقدمتاه إما كليّة وإما أن تكون إحداهما جزئية، فإن كانتا كليتين فالكذب إما فيهما وإما في إحداهما، فإن كان فيهما فإما في كل واحدة منهما وإما في بعضها، وإما في كل إحداهما وبعض الآخر، وإن كان الكذب في كل واحدة منهما فمثاله من الشكل الأول وهما موجبتان هو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الطرفين بالسلب الكلي، كقولنا: (كل إنسان جماد) و(كل جماد حيوان)، ومثاله منه والكبرى سالبة هو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الأصغر بالسلب الكلي، ونسبة الأكبر إلى الأوسط بالإيجاب الكلي، كقولنا: (كل جماد إنسان) و(لا شيء من الإنسان حيوان) فـ (لا شيء من الجماد حيوان). وإن كان من الشكل الثاني فمثاله والكبرى فيه سالبة، هو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الأصغر بالسلب، وإلى الأكبر بالإيجاب الكلي، كقولنا: (كل فرس إنسان) و(لا شيء من الضحاك إنساناً) فـ (لا شيء من الفرس ضاحك)، ومثاله ولتكن الصغرى سالبة، هو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الطرفين على عکس من الأول، كقولنا: (لا شيء من الإنسان ناطق) و(كل فرس ناطق) فـ (لا شيء من الإنسان فرس). وإن كان من الشكل الثالث فمثاله والمقدمتان موجبتان هو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الطرفين المختلفين بالسلب الكلي، كقولنا: (كل جماد إنسان) و(كل جماد ناطق) فـ (بعض الإنسان ناطق) ومثاله ولتكن الكبرى منه سالبة، وهو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الأصغر إلى الأوسط بالسلب الكلي، ونسبة الأكبر بالإيجاب الكلي، كقولنا: (كل ققنس أسود) و(لا شيء من الققنس حيوان) فـ (بعض الأسود ليس بحيوان). وإن كان من الشكل الرابع فمثاله ولتكن المقدمتان موجبتين، هو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الأصغر إلى الأوسط، والأوسط إلى الأكبر بالسلب الكلي، كقولنا: (كل جماد إنسان) و(كل ناطق جماد) فـ (بعض الإنسان ناطق) ومثاله ولتكن الصغرى منه سالبة ما الصادق فيه نسبة الحد الأصغر إلى الأوسط بالإيجاب الكلي، والأوسط إلى الأكبر بالسلب الكلي، كقولنا: (لا شيء من الإنسان ناطق) و(كل فرس إنسان) فـ (لا شيء من الناطق فرس) ومثاله ولتكن الكبرى سالبة، هو ما الصادق نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الطرفين على عكس المثال الذي قبله كقولنا: (كل جماد إنسان) و(لا شيء من الحجر جماد) فـ (بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر). وأما إن كان الكذب في بعض كل واحد منهما، فمثاله من الشكل الأول، والمقدمتان موجبتان هو ما الصادق فيه نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الأصغر بالسلب الجزئي، ونسبة الأكبر إلى الأوسط بالإيجاب الجزئي، كقولنا: (كل إنسان أبيض) و(كل أبيض حيوان) فـ (كل إنسان حيوان) ومثاله ولتكن الكبرى سالبة: قولنا: (كل إنسان أبيض) و(لا شيء من الأبيض حجر) فـ (لا شيء من الإنسان حجر). وإن كان من الشكل الثاني، فمثاله والكبرى سالبة قولنا: (كل إنسان أبيض) و(لا شيء من الفرس أبيض) فـ (لا شيء من الإنسان فرس)، ومثاله ولتكن الصغرى سالبة ما لو جعلت الصغرى في هذا المثال كبرى والكبرى صغرى، فيلزم أن لا شيء من الفرس إنسان. ومثال من الشكل الثالث، ولتكن المقدمتان موجبتين قولنا كل جسم حيوان وكل جسم إنسان فـ (بعض الحيوان إنسان).
It is not part of the subject nor of the predicate, such as the quantifier and modality and the like. So our saying: 'Man alone is rational' indicates the affirmation of 'rational' for 'man' and its negation from other than man. And its equivalent is our saying: 'Man is rational' (non-man is not rational). And the subject for the affirmation of 'rational' for it is only 'man' considered without the 'alone'; so our saying: 'Man is rational' is true when abstracted from 'alone'. Thus at the time of syllogistic production, it is necessary to abstract the minor term and the major term from everything that has no entry in them. The minor term is 'man', and the major is 'body'. It is not hidden that the relation of 'body' to 'man' is true and not false. And what the 'alone' indicates, of negating 'rational' from non-man, has no entry in this syllogism. (Then we return to where we stopped and say:) Every conjunction (iqtiran), its two premises are either both universal or one of them is particular. If both are universal, then the falsehood is either in both or in one of them. If it is in both, then either in each one of them or in some of each, or in all of one and some of the other. If the falsehood is in each one of them, then an example from the first figure (both affirmative) is that in which the truth is the relation of the middle term to the extremes by universal negation, like our saying: 'Every man is an inanimate object' and 'Every inanimate object is an animal' (false). An example from it with the major premise negative is that in which the truth is the relation of the middle to the minor by universal negation and the relation of the major to the middle by universal affirmation, like our saying: 'Every inanimate object is a man' and 'No man is an animal', so 'No inanimate object is an animal'. If it is from the second figure (with the major negative), an example is that in which the truth is the relation of the middle to the minor by negation and to the major by universal affirmation, like our saying: 'Every horse is a man' and 'No one who laughs is a man', so 'No horse is a laugher'. An example with the minor premise negative is that in which the truth is the relation of the middle to the two extremes opposite to the first, like our saying: 'No man is rational' and 'Every horse is rational', so 'No man is a horse'. If it is from the third figure (both affirmative), an example is that in which the truth is the relation of the middle to the two extremes (different) by universal negation, like our saying: 'Every inanimate object is a man' and 'Every inanimate object is rational', so 'Some man is rational'. An example with the major negative is that in which the truth is the relation of the minor to the middle by universal negation and the relation of the major by universal affirmation, like our saying: 'Every phoenix is black' and 'No phoenix is an animal', so 'Some black thing is not an animal'. If it is from the fourth figure (both affirmative), an example is that in which the truth is the relation of the minor to the middle and the middle to the major by universal negation, like our saying: 'Every inanimate object is a man' and 'Every rational is inanimate', so 'Some man is rational'. An example with the minor negative is that in which the truth is the relation of the minor to the middle by universal affirmation and the middle to the major by universal negation, like our saying: 'No man is rational' and 'Every horse is a man', so 'No rational is a horse'. An example with the major negative is that in which the truth is the relation of the middle to the two extremes opposite to the previous example, like our saying: 'Every inanimate object is a man' and 'No stone is inanimate', so 'Some man is not a stone'. As for when the falsehood is in some part of each of them, an example from the first figure (both affirmative) is that in which the truth is the relation of the middle to the minor by particular negation and the relation of the major to the middle by particular affirmation, like our saying: 'Every man is white' and 'Everything white is an animal', so 'Every man is an animal'. An example with the major negative: our saying: 'Every man is white' and 'Nothing white is a stone', so 'No man is a stone'. If it is from the second figure, an example with the major negative: our saying: 'Every man is white' and 'No horse is white', so 'No man is a horse'. An example with the minor negative: if the minor in this example is made the major and the major the minor, then it follows that 'No horse is a man'. An example from the third figure (both affirmative): our saying: 'Every body is an animal' and 'Every body is a man', so 'Some animal is a man'.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the conditions under which falsehood occurs in categorical syllogisms (al-qiyas al-iqtirani) across the four standard figures (ashkal). The author begins by emphasizing the need to strip the minor and major terms of any extraneous elements—such as quantifiers (al-sawar) or modalities (al-jiha)—before the syllogistic production, because only the bare subject and predicate are relevant to the relation of the middle term. For example, the phrase 'man alone is rational' includes the word 'alone' which implies a negation of 'rational' from non-man, but that nuance is irrelevant when constructing a syllogism; the proper form is 'man is rational'. Similarly, in a syllogism where the minor term is 'man' and the major 'body', the relation 'body is man' is true, but the added 'alone' has no function. Then the author returns to the main topic: every conjunction (qiyas iqtirani) has two premises that are either both universal or one particular. Focusing on the universal case, falsehood can be total or partial. The author gives examples for each figure (four figures) when both premises are false (kadhib fi kulli wahida minhuma) and also when falsehood is partial (fi ba'd kulli wahid minhuma). For each figure, he provides cases with both premises affirmative and with one premise negative. For instance, in the first figure with both affirmative, falsehood occurs when the middle term is related to both extremes by universal negation, e.g., 'Every man is inanimate; every inanimate is an animal' (both false). With a negative major, the middle is universally negated of the minor and universally affirmed of the major, e.g., 'Every inanimate is a man; no man is an animal' → 'No inanimate is an animal'. Similar patterns are given for the second, third, and fourth figures, careful to adjust the order of terms (al-saghir, al-akbar, al-awsat). In the second figure, the middle is the predicate in both premises; in the third, it is the subject in both; in the fourth, it is the predicate in the first premise and subject in the second. The author then covers the case of partial falsehood (kadhib fi ba'd kulli wahid minhuma) with examples, again per figure, showing how the pattern changes when only part of the premise is false (e.g., some men are white, some white things are animals, but not all; this yields a true conclusion but the premises are not universally true). This enumeration is crucial for the logician to identify fallacious syllogisms by inspecting the truth-values of the premises.

الحد الأصغر
الحد الأكبر
السور
الجهة

According to the text, what is the correct logical form of the proposition 'Man alone is rational' for syllogistic purposes?

B) Man is rational
