Falsity in the Universal Major Premise Across All Figures
Author illustrates syllogisms where the universal major premise is false, either wholly or partially, across all four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism's universal major premise is wholly false and determine the formal validity and material soundness of the conclusion across all four figures.

Core Idea
A false universal major premise can yield a formally valid but materially unsound syllogism.

In classical logic, a syllogism's validity depends on its form, not the truth of its premises. The author examines what happens when the universal major premise is completely false. By working through examples in all four figures, you will see how a false premise can still generate a conclusion that is accidentally true or false. This sharpens your ability to distinguish between formal validity and material soundness—a critical skill in evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of falsity
The author first distinguishes between falsity in the entire major premise (al-kidhb fī kullihā) and falsity in part of it. Here we focus on total falsity.
Total falsity means the universal statement is completely false, e.g., 'Every human is a horse'.
2
First figure examples
The author provides two examples: one with a universal affirmative major ('Every human is a horse') and one with a universal negative major ('No human is rational'). The minor is particular affirmative: 'Some animals are humans'. In both cases, the conclusion follows validly (Darii and Ferio moods).
Note that 'No human is rational' is false because humans are rational.
3
Second figure examples
Here the minor premise can be particular affirmative or particular negative, and the major is universal negative or affirmative respectively. The examples show that the conclusion is a particular negative: 'Some humans are not horses'.
Second figure requires the middle term to be the predicate of both premises.
4
Third and fourth figures
The third figure repeats the pattern with universal majors and particular minors. The fourth figure example is cut off, but it follows the same logic: the major is universal negative, the minor is particular affirmative, yielding a particular conclusion.
Despite the incomplete fourth example, the structure is clear.
5
Evaluate soundness
For each example, check the truth value of premises and conclusion. E.g., 'Every human is a horse' is false, 'Some animals are humans' is true, and the conclusion 'Some animals are horses' is false. The syllogism is valid but unsound.
Some conclusions may be accidentally true, e.g., 'Some animals are not rational' is true, but the premise 'No human is rational' is false.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Validity vs Truth in Syllogisms
Systematic examples of syllogisms with false premises across all four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure and mood of a categorical syllogism and determine whether it is valid regardless of the truth of its premises.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism can have false premises; logical form and truth are distinct.

This passage from Daqaiq Haqaiq presents a rich set of examples that drive home a critical distinction: a syllogism may be formally valid even if its premises are false. As you work through the examples, you will learn to separate the structure of an argument from the factual accuracy of its content—a skill essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where a weak premise can still yield a valid form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
The author explicitly names the figure (first, second, third, fourth) for each example. Note the arrangement of the middle term in the premises.
2
Extract the mood
Determine the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of each premise. The example 'Some human is stone; No stone is a horse; therefore some human is not a horse' has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major.
Mood is expressed by three letters (e.g., AEO).
3
Check validity conditions
For each figure, recall the rules (e.g., first figure requires universal major, affirmative minor, etc.). The passage shows that even with false premises, the same formal conditions apply.
Validity depends only on form, not truth.
4
Notice the falsehood pattern
The author deliberately uses false statements (e.g., 'Every human is a horse') to show that the syllogism still 'produces' a conclusion. This reinforces the independence of logical form from truth.
The conclusion may also be false, but that does not affect validity.

And an example from the second figure, let the minor be particular affirmative and the major universal negative: (Some human is stone) and (No horse is stone) so (Some human is not a horse). And an example, let the minor be negative: (Some human is not an animal) and (Every horse is an animal) so (Some human is not a horse). And an example from the third figure, let the major be universal affirmative: (Some human is stone) and (Every human is a body) so (Some stone is a body). And an example, let the major be universal negative: (Some human is stone) and (No human is a horse) so (Some stone is not a horse). And an example from the fourth figure, let its major be universal negative, and its minor particular affirmative: (Some human is stone) and (No horse is human) so (Some stone is not a horse). As for if the falsehood is in the universal major, then either the falsehood is in all of it or part of it. If it is in all of it, then an example from the first figure, let the major be universal affirmative: (Some animal is human) and (Every human is a horse) so (Some animal is a horse). And an example from it, let the major be universal negative: (Some animal is human) and (No human is rational) so (Some animal is not rational). And an example from the second figure, let the minor be particular affirmative and the major universal negative: (Some human is animal) and (No horse is animal) so (Some human is not a horse). And an example, let the minor be particular negative and the major universal affirmative: (Some animal is not human) and (Every horse is human) so (Some animal is not a horse). And an example from the third figure, let the major be universal affirmative: (Some human is animal) and (Every human is a horse) so (Some animal is a horse). And an example, let the major be universal negative: (Some human is animal) and (No human is rational) so (Some animal is not rational). And an example from the fourth figure, where the minor is particular affirmative and its major universal negative: (Some human is animal) and (No laughing being is human) so (Some animal is not a laughing being). As for if the falsehood of the major is in part not all, then an example from the first figure, let the major be affirmative: (Some human is animal) and (Every animal is rational) so (Some human is rational). And an example, let the major be negative: (Some human is animal) and (No animal is a horse) so (Some human is not a horse). And an example from the second figure, let the minor be affirmative and the major negative: (Some raven is black) and (No human is black) so (Some raven is not human). And an example, let the minor be negative and the major affirmative: (Some raven is not white) and (Every human is white) so (Some raven is not human). And an example from the third figure, let the major be affirmative: (Some human is animal) and (Every human is white) so (Some animal is white). And an example, let the major be negative: (Some human is animal) and (No human is white) so (Some animal is not white). And an example from the fourth figure, where its minor is particular affirmative and its major universal negative: (Some human is animal) and (No white thing is human) so (Some animal is not white). As for if the particular is the major and the universal is the minor, then there is no production from the first and second figures, because of the condition that their major be universal; only from the third and fourth does it produce. And then the falsehood is either in both premises or one of them. If it is in both premises universally, then either the falsehood is universal in both or partial. If it is universal in both, then an example from the third figure, let the particular be affirmative: (Every human is a horse) and (Some human is neighing) so (Some horse is neighing). And an example, let the major be negative: (Every human is stone) and (Some human is not animal) so (Some stone is not animal). And an example from the fourth figure, where the particular is affirmative and the minor is universal affirmative: (Every human is stone) and (Some inanimate object is human) so (Some stone is inanimate). Commentary
This passage from Daqaiq Haqaiq presents a systematic enumeration of categorical syllogism examples across the four figures, specifically focusing on cases where one or both premises are false. The author first gives examples from the second, third, and fourth figures with a particular minor and universal major, without specifying falsehood—likely illustrating the form. Then, a new section addresses falsehood in the universal major, dividing into complete falsity (all of the major premise is false) and partial falsity (some part false). For each subcase, examples are given from each of the four figures. For instance, in the first figure with a false universal affirmative major: 'Some animal is human; every human is a horse; therefore some animal is a horse.' Note that the major premise 'every human is a horse' is false, but the syllogism is valid in form. The same pattern is repeated for the second, third, and fourth figures with both affirmative and negative majors. The text then treats partial falsity of the major, again with examples from all figures. Finally, the author considers the case where the major premise is particular and the minor universal, noting that only the third and fourth figures produce a conclusion (since the first and second require a universal major). Examples are provided for the third and fourth figures with falsehood in both premises. The footnote corrects a textual error: 'كل أبيض إنسان' should be 'كل إنسان أبيض' (Every human is white). Throughout, the focus is on the formal structure of syllogisms, demonstrating that logical validity is independent of truth value. In Islamic logic, such examples help students distinguish between form (sura) and matter (madda), a key distinction in evaluating arguments. For instance, the example 'Every human is a horse' parallels a false universal in fiqh debates where a faulty generalization leads to an invalid ruling. The use of concrete terms like 'human', 'horse', 'rational', 'laughing being' reflects the typical examples in the Aristotelian tradition adapted into Arabic logic.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism can be valid even with false premises; logical form is independent of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the figure and mood of this kalam syllogism: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Is it valid? Are the premises true?
?
Construct a valid categorical syllogism with false premises on a fiqh topic (e.g., all animals are humans, some horse is animal, so some horse is human). Then state whether the conclusion is true or false.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
When Major Premises Are Particular: Which Figures Deliver?
Conditions for conclusion with particular major premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which figures in a categorical syllogism produce a valid conclusion when the major premise is particular and the minor is universal.

Core Idea
Only third and fourth figures handle particular major premises; first and second require a universal major.

In the standard categorical syllogism, the first and second figures require a universal major premise for a valid conclusion. But what happens when the major is particular? The author shows that only the third and fourth figures can still produce a conclusion in this case. This rule is essential for correctly evaluating syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with arguments that use particular premises in theological or juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor premises
In the syllogism, locate the major premise (containing the predicate of the conclusion) and the minor premise (containing the subject). Determine whether each is universal or particular.
Remember: 'kubra' is major, 'sughra' is minor.
2
Check the figure
Determine which figure the syllogism belongs to by the position of the middle term. The first and second figures have the middle as subject of the major and predicate of the minor (first) or predicate of both (second). The third and fourth have different arrangements.
Refer to the standard four figures.
3
Apply the rule
If the major premise is particular and the minor is universal, only the third and fourth figures yield a conclusion. The first and second figures do not, because they require a universal major.
The author explicitly states: 'لا إنتاج من الشكل الأول والثاني... وإنما ينتج من الثالث والرابع'.
4
Test with an example
Take the valid example from the fourth figure: 'Some human is animal' (particular major) and 'No white is human' (universal minor) gives 'Some animal is not white'. Confirm that this is indeed a valid syllogism in the fourth figure.
Example from the text: بالشكل الرابع (بعض الإنسان حيوان) و (لا شيء من الأبيض إنسان) فـ (بعض الحيوان ليس أبيض).

And as for if the falsehood of the major is in some (parts) not all, an example from the first figure: let the major be affirmative: 'Some human is animal' and 'Every animal is rational' so 'Some human is rational'. And an example: let the major be negative: 'Some human is animal' and 'No animal is a horse' so 'Some human is not a horse'.
And an example from the second figure: let the minor be affirmative and the major negative: 'Some crow is black' and 'No human is black' so 'Some crow is not a human'. And an example: let the minor be negative and the major affirmative: 'Some crow is not white' and 'Every human is white' so 'Some crow is not a human'.

And an example from the third figure: let the major be affirmative: 'Some human is animal' and 'Every human is white' so 'Some animal is white'. And an example: let the major be negative: 'Some human is animal' and 'No human is white' so 'Some animal is not white'.
And an example from the fourth figure: it is that whose minor is a particular affirmative and its major is a universal negative, like our saying: 'Some human is animal' and 'No white is human' so 'Some animal is not white'. And as for if the particular is the major and the universal is the minor, then there is no production from the first and second figures because of the condition of universality of their major, and rather it produces from the third and fourth. And in that case, the falsehood is either in both premises or one of them. So if it is in both premises, then the falsehood is either in the universal in both of them or in some of it. And if it is in all of it, an example from the third figure: let the particular be affirmative: 'Every human is a horse' and 'Some human is neighing' so 'Some horse is neighing'. And an example: let the major be negative: 'Every human is a stone' and 'Some human is not an animal' so 'Some stone is not an animal'.
And an example from the fourth figure: it is that whose particular is affirmative and its minor is universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Every human is a stone' and 'Some inanimate is human' so 'Some stone is inanimate'.

Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part Two / Continuation of the Fourth Treatise on Defining the Forms of Arguments
And as for if the universal is false in some but not all, an example from the third figure: let the particular be affirmative: 'Every human is white' and 'Some human is a horse' so 'Some white is a horse'. And an example: let the particular be negative: 'Every human is white' and 'Some human is not rational' so 'Some white is not rational'.
And an example from the fourth figure: let both premises be affirmative: 'Every human is white' and 'Some qaqnas is human' so 'Some white is qaqnas'. And as for if one of them is false and the other true, then either the false is the particular or the universal. If it is the particular, an example from the third figure: let the particular be affirmative: 'Every human is animal' and 'Some human is a horse' so 'Some animal is a horse'. And an example: let the particular be negative: 'Every human is animal' and 'Some human is not rational' so 'The animal is not rational'.
And an example from the fourth figure: 'Every human is animal' and 'Some horse is human' so 'Some animal is a horse'. And as for if the universal is false, then it is either false in all or some. If it is false in all, an example from the third figure: let the particular be affirmative: 'Every human is a horse' and 'Some human is animal' so 'Some horse is animal'. And an example: let the particular be negative: 'Every human is a horse' and 'Some (human) is not a donkey' so 'Some horse is not a donkey'. And an example from the fourth figure: 'Every crow is white' and 'Some animal is a crow' so 'Some white is animal'.
And as for if it is false in some, an example from the third figure: let the particular be affirmative: 'Every human is white' and 'Some human is animal' so 'Some white is animal'. And an example: let the particular be negative: 'Every human is white' and 'Some human is not a horse' so 'Some white is not a horse'.
And an example from the fourth figure: 'Every human is white' and 'Some animal is human' so 'Some white is animal'. And you must consider that in the hypothetical (syllogism).
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of categorical syllogisms with particular premises and the effects of false premises. The author first treats the case where the major premise is false in some but not all, providing examples from each of the four figures. For the first figure, he gives two examples: one with an affirmative major and one with a negative major. For the second figure, he gives examples with different combinations of affirmative and negative premises. For the third figure, he gives examples with affirmative and negative majors. For the fourth figure, he specifies that the minor must be particular affirmative and the major universal negative to produce a conclusion. He then states a key rule: when the particular premise is the major and the universal is the minor, no conclusion follows in the first and second figures because they require a universal major; only the third and fourth figures produce a conclusion. This is a vital point for valid syllogistic reasoning in these figures.

The author then turns to the case where premises are false. He distinguishes whether the falsehood is in both premises or one, and whether it is universal or particular falsehood. He provides numerous examples from the third and fourth figures (since they are the ones that can handle particular premises). For instance, if both premises are universally false, the conclusion may still be true, as in the example: 'Every human is a horse' (false) and 'Some human is neighing' (true) leading to 'Some horse is neighing' (true in the sense that some horse does neigh). Similarly, he covers cases where the universal is false in part, where one premise is false and the other true, and the various combinations. The passage ends with a note that similar consideration applies to hypothetical syllogisms.

All examples use terms familiar from logic textbooks: humans, animals, horses, crows, rational, white, etc. The key lesson is that the truth of the conclusion does not depend solely on the truth of premises; logical validity ensures that if premises are true, conclusion must be true, but false premises can still yield a true conclusion by accident.

False Premises and Logical Validity – Do They Always Invalidate?
Effects of false premises on syllogistic conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the types of falsehood in premises of a categorical syllogism and determine whether a conclusion follows, regardless of the truth of premises.

Core Idea
False premises can still yield a true conclusion; logical validity depends on form, not factual truth.

A common misconception is that a false premise automatically makes the conclusion false. In deductive logic, however, the validity of a syllogism is independent of the truth of its premises. The author examines various cases where one or both premises are false, yet a conclusion still follows (sometimes true, sometimes false). Understanding this distinction is critical for sound argumentation, especially in Islamic legal reasoning where premises may be probabilistic or contested.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of falsehood
Determine whether the falsehood is in both premises or just one, and whether it is universal or partial falsehood. The author distinguishes: false in all, false in some, false in one premise.
The Arabic terms: 'كذب في الكل' (false in all), 'كذب في البعض' (false in some).
2
Check the figure
The examples focus on the third and fourth figures, since those are the ones that can handle particular premises. Determine which figure the syllogism belongs to.
The author states: 'وعند ذلك فالكذب إما في المقدمتين أو إحداهما'.
3
Apply the rule of entailment
Even if premises are false, the syllogism may still be valid (i.e., if premises were true, conclusion would be true). The author's examples show that false premises can lead to a true conclusion (e.g., 'Every human is a horse' false, 'Some human is neighing' false? Actually 'Some human is neighing' is true? Neighing is a property of horses, so false premise but true conclusion?). Actually the example: 'Every human is a horse' (false) and 'Some human is neighing' (true?) – but if all humans are horses, then some human is neighing is true, but the premise is false. So conclusion is true. This shows validity can coexist with false premises.
The point is not about truth but about logical form.
4
Test with a false premise combination
Take an example from the passage: 'Every human is a stone' (false) and 'Some human is not an animal' (false) leads to 'Some stone is not an animal' (true? Actually some stone is not animal is true). Confirm that the syllogism is valid in form (third figure, FERIO? Actually it's third figure: major 'No animal is human'? Wait, the example in translation: 'Every human is a stone' and 'Some human is not an animal' so 'Some stone is not an animal'. This is valid in third figure if we analyze the mood.
Focus on the form, not the factual accuracy.

False Premises in Third and Fourth Figure Syllogisms
Author enumerates cases of false premises in third and fourth figures with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify cases of false universal and particular premises in third and fourth figure categorical syllogisms and predict the truth value of the conclusion.

Core Idea
False premises do not guarantee a false conclusion; logical form determines validity, not truth.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates scenarios where premises in categorical syllogisms are false, specifically in the third and fourth figures. You will learn to distinguish between universal falsity (in all or some) and particular falsity, and see how the conclusion's truth value is independent of premise truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check if the syllogism is in the third or fourth figure based on the middle term position.
Third figure: middle term is subject of both premises. Fourth figure: middle term is predicate of first and subject of second.
2
Classify the false premise
Determine whether the false premise is universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya).
The passage covers both cases.
3
For false universal, subdivide
If the universal is false, decide if it is false in all (kādhiba fī al-kull) or in some (fī al-ba'd).
This distinction affects the example.
4
Locate the corresponding example
Using the classification, find the example given in the passage for that combination.
Examples are provided for each permutation.
5
Observe the conclusion's truth value
Note that the conclusion may be true even if a premise is false, as in the case where the universal is true and the particular is false yet the conclusion is true.
This illustrates that truth of conclusion does not follow from truth of premises alone.

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic enumeration of cases where premises in categorical syllogisms are false, specifically in the third and fourth figures. The author first considers the case where the universal premise is false in some instances but not all. He gives examples: in the third figure, with an affirmative particular ('Every human is white' and 'Some human is a horse' yields 'Some white is a horse') and with a negative particular ('Some human is not rational' yields 'Some white is not rational'). In the fourth figure, with both premises affirmative, 'Every human is white' and 'Some horse is human' yields 'Some white is a horse'. Next, the author treats the case where one premise is false and the other true, subdivided by whether the false premise is particular or universal. For false particular, examples from the third figure show that even with a false particular, the conclusion may still be true (e.g., 'Every human is animal' true, 'Some human is a horse' false, yet 'Some animal is a horse' is false? Actually the example seems to yield a true conclusion? The author does not comment on truth value of conclusion, just the form. For false universal, further split into false in all or false in some. Examples are provided for each subcase. The passage ends by noting that similar considerations apply to conditional syllogisms. This systematic approach helps the student understand how false premises affect syllogistic validity and conclusion truth.

Key Terms
كلية (kulliyya): A universal proposition (e.g., 'Every human is white').
جزئية (juz'iyya): A particular proposition (e.g., 'Some human is a horse').
شكل ثالث (shakl thalith): Third figure of categorical syllogism (middle term is subject of both premises).
شكل رابع (shakl rabi'): Fourth figure of categorical syllogism (middle term is predicate of first premise and subject of second). [Note: The fourth figure is often considered a variant of the first figure in some texts; here it is treated distinctly.]

Checkpoint
In the third figure, if the universal premise 'Every human is a horse' is false in all, and the particular premise 'Some human is animal' is true, what is the conclusion?
A) Some horse is animal
B) Every horse is animal
C) Some animal is a horse
D) No animal is a horse

Key Takeaway
In categorical syllogisms, false premises can still produce a valid argument and a true conclusion; truth of premises and validity are independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What is the conclusion? Is it true or false? (Hint: Use the example pattern from the passage with 'human' and 'horse' replaced by 'intoxicant' and 'wine'.)

The Syllogism

Distinguishing Intuitive from Theoretical Propositions
Author classifies propositions as intuitive or theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given proposition as intuitive (badihi) or theoretical (nazari) based on its need for syllogistic demonstration.

Core Idea
Intuitive propositions are self-evident; theoretical propositions require a systematic search.

In this passage, the author begins by distinguishing two types of tasdiqi meanings: intuitive and theoretical. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it tells us which propositions need a syllogism and which do not. The method for acquiring premises only applies to theoretical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
Take a proposition you want to examine (e.g., 'The world is temporally originated'). Determine if it is self-evident or requires proof.
2
Check for self-evidence
If the proposition's truth is clear without needing any demonstration, it is badihi. If not, it is nazari.
Examples of badihi: 'The whole is greater than the part', 'Contradictories cannot both be true'.
3
Classify accordingly
For nazari propositions, the method of acquiring premises described in this lesson applies. For badihi, no search is needed.

الفصل الخامس
في اكتساب المقدمات وكيفية تحصيل أقيسة المطلوبات
وإذ قد فرغنا من بيان الأقيسة، وتعريف أصنافها، فجدير بنا تكميل الانتفاع بتعريف طرق اكتسابها على وجه كلي لا من حيث هو برهاني، أو خطابي، أو جدلي، أو غير ذلك كما يأتي، إذ قل ما ينتفع بمعرفة حقيقة القياس عند إرادة استعماله من لا يعرف طريق تحصيله، إذ يجده محصلاً.
فنقول: المعاني التصديقية إما بديهية أو نظرية فإن كانت بديهية فلا حاجة إلى طلبها، فإن ما يتبين به ليس بأبين منها، وافتقار بعضها إن كان فليس إلا إلى أدنى تذكر وتنبيه كما سبق.
وإن كانت نظرية فكيفية طريق اكتساب كل مطلوب منها أن تضع أولاً إحدى المطلوب ثم تنظر في حد كل واحد واحد من الحدّين، وما يلحق بكل واحد منهما من الذاتيات والعرضيات، وذاتيات الذاتيات والعرضيات وعرضيات كل واحد منها على جهة الاستقصاء، ثم تنظر في ما ينسب إلى كل واحد، وما ينسب هو إليه سلباً أو إيجاباً، ولا تبال كانت النسبة طبيعية كنسبة العارض إلى موضوعه أو بالعكس، أو كنسبة عارض إلى عارض، وعلى حسب زيادة البحث عن هذه الأمور تكون زيادة الانتفاع، وإياك والبحث هاهنا عن لاحق اللاحق هل هو لاحق؟ أو عن ملحوق الملحوق هل هو ملحوق؟ إذ هو استعمال بما هو معلوم، أو أن تشتغل بالبحث عما يلحق الحدّين، إلا أن تختلف نسبته إليهما بالضرورة والإمكان أو غير ذلك مما حققناه في الشكل الثاني أو أن تشتغل بما لا يلحق بهما، لعدم جدواه كما مضى، أو تطلب التضاد بين لواحق الحدين، أو التغاير بينهما، لتبني عليه سلب أحد الحدين عن الآخر، كما قد يتخيل متخيل، وهو كما يقال: (النار حارة والماء بارد أو أن هذا حيوان وهذا (جماد) إذ الحد الأوسط يجب أن يكون واحداً، ولولا أن ما هو حار ليس ببارد وما هو بارد ليس بحار وأن ما هو حيوان ليس بجماد) و(ما هو جماد ليس بحيوان وإلا لما لزم القياس، فانعقاد القياس في مثل هذه الأمور إن كان، فليس إلا لأن ما لحق "ج" مثلاً غير لاحق لـ "ب" وبالعكس، لا لما لحق "ج" و"ب".
وبعد الفراغ عن البحث في هذه الأمور فالواجب أن ننظر في ما يناسب المطلوب من هذه القضايا فما لم نجده مناسباً للمطلوب أو لأحـد حـديـه فإياك والاشتغال به؛ إذ هو عديم الجدوى، وما يناسب فإما أن يكون مناسباً لكليته بأن يكون منضماً له، أو لنقيضه، أو لأحد حديه، فإن كانت مناسبته لأحد حديه فعليك بطلب ما يناسب الحد الآخر، فإن وجدت فإما أن يكون بين النقيضين المناسبتين لحدي المطلوب اشتراك في حد آخر أو ليس فإن كان ليس فلا انتفاع بهما في المطلوب، وإن كان فإن كان مطلوبك كلياً موجباً وكان الحد المشترك على نمط الأول من الشكل الأول مقترناً بشرائط فقد يتم قياسك وإلا فلا وإن كان مطلوبك كلياً سالباً وكان الحد المشترك على نمط الاقتران الثاني من الأول، أو الأول والثاني من الشكل الثاني أو الأول من الرابع، وكل واحد مقترن فقد تم وإلا فلا، وإن كان مطلوبك جزئياً موجباً وكان الحد الأوسط على نمط الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول، أو الأول والثاني والثالث من الشكل الثالث أو الثاني والثالث من الشكل الرابع، وكل واحد قد اقترنت به شرائطه فقد تم القياس أيضاً، وإن كان مطلوبك جزئياً سالباً وكان الحد الأوسط على نمط الاقتران الرابع من الأول، أو الثالث والرابع من الشكل الثاني أو الرابع والخامس والسادس من الثالث، أو الرابع والخامس من الرابع، وكان كل واحد قد اقترنت به شرائطه فقد تم القياس أيضاً وإلا فلا.
وعند ذلك فتعلم أن ما يتبين من المطالب بشكل واحد فقط أصعب مما يتبين بأشكال عدة، بل وما يتبين بضرب واحد من الشكل فهو أصعب مما يتبين بضروب من اقترانات ذلك الشكل أو شكل غيره، والكلي الموجب لا يتبين لغير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول، فإثباته أعسر من غيره، بل وإثباته أعسر من إبطاله؛ لتوقف الإثبات على وجه واحد والإبطال فقد يكون ببيان النقيض، وذلك في الأشكال
Chapter Five: On Acquiring Premises and How to Obtain Syllogisms for Desired Conclusions. Since we have finished explaining syllogisms and defining their types, it is fitting for us to complete the benefit by defining the methods of acquiring them in a general way, not in terms of being demonstrative, rhetorical, dialectical, or otherwise, as will come, for rarely does one benefit from knowing the reality of syllogism when wanting to use it without knowing the method of obtaining it, since he finds it obtained. We say: Tasdiqi meanings are either intuitive or theoretical. If they are intuitive, there is no need to seek them, for what clarifies them is not clearer than them, and if some depend, it is only on the slightest recollection and reminder, as previously mentioned. If they are theoretical, then the method of acquiring each desired conclusion from them is that you first set up one of the desired conclusions, then examine the definition of each one of the two terms, and what attaches to each of them from essential and accidental attributes, and the essentials of essentials and accidents, and the accidents of each of them exhaustively. Then examine what is attributed to each, and what each is attributed to, negatively or affirmatively, not minding whether the relation is natural like the relation of an accident to its subject or vice versa, or like the relation of an accident to an accident. According to the extent of investigation into these matters, the benefit increases. Beware of investigating here whether the attribute of an attribute is an attribute? Or whether the subject of a subject is a subject? For that is using what is already known. Or that you busy yourself with investigating what attaches to the two terms, unless their relation to them differs by necessity or possibility or other than that which we verified in the second figure, or that you busy yourself with what does not attach to them, due to its uselessness as has passed. Or you seek contradiction between the attributes of the two terms, or difference between them, to base on it the negation of one term from the other, as some might imagine, and it is as it is said: (Fire is hot and water is cold, or that this is an animal and this is inanimate) because the middle term must be one, and were it not that what is hot is not cold and what is cold is not hot, and that what is animal is not inanimate, and what is inanimate is not animal, otherwise the syllogism would not be valid. So the validity of the syllogism in such matters, if it exists, is only because what attaches to 'j', for example, is not an attribute of 'b', and vice versa, not because of what attaches to 'j' and 'b'. After finishing the investigation of these matters, it is obligatory to look at what is suitable for the desired conclusion among these propositions. Whatever we do not find suitable for the desired conclusion or for one of its two terms, then beware of occupying yourself with it, for it is useless. And what is suitable is either suitable for its entirety by being joined to it, or to its opposite, or to one of its two terms. If its suitability is for one of its two terms, then you must seek what is suitable for the other term. If you find, then either there is between the two opposites that are suitable for the two terms of the desired conclusion a common third term or not. If there is not, then no benefit from them in the desired conclusion. If there is, then if your desired conclusion is universal affirmative, and the common term is in the pattern of the first mood of the first figure, accompanied by its conditions, then your syllogism is complete, otherwise not. If your desired conclusion is universal negative, and the common term is in the pattern of the second mood of the first, or the first and second of the second figure, or the first of the fourth, each one being accompanied, then it is complete, otherwise not. If your desired conclusion is particular affirmative, and the middle term is in the pattern of the third mood of the first figure, or the first, second, and third of the third figure, or the second and third of the fourth figure, each one having its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete. If your desired conclusion is particular negative, and the middle term is in the pattern of the fourth mood of the first, or the third and fourth of the second, or the fourth, fifth, and sixth of the third, or the fourth and fifth of the fourth, and each one has its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete, otherwise not. At that point, know that what is clarified by only one figure is more difficult than what is clarified by several figures. Rather, what is clarified by one mood of a figure is more difficult than what is clarified by moods of that figure or another figure. The universal affirmative is not clarified except by the first mood of the first figure, so proving it is more difficult than others. Rather, proving it is more difficult than disproving it, because proving depends on one way, while disproving may be by demonstrating the opposite, and that is in the figures.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage presents the method for acquiring premises to construct a syllogism for a desired conclusion in logic. The author begins by distinguishing between intuitive (badihi) and theoretical (nazari) tasdiqi meanings. Intuitive propositions require no search, while theoretical ones need a systematic procedure. The method: first, set up the desired conclusion (matlub) and analyze each of its two terms (haddain) thoroughly, examining their essential and accidental attributes exhaustively. Then examine the relations of attribution (positive or negative) between these attributes and the terms. The author warns against useless investigations, such as whether an attribute of an attribute is itself an attribute, or seeking contradiction or difference between the attributes of the two terms without a proper middle term. He illustrates with an example: 'Fire is hot and water is cold' or 'This is an animal and this is inanimate'—the middle term must be one, and the negation of one attribute from the other is not enough; the syllogism works only because the attributes of 'j' and 'b' are mutually exclusive. After this preparatory analysis, the next step is to find propositions that are suitable (munasib) for the desired conclusion or its terms. If a proposition suits one term, seek one that suits the other. If there is common term between them, then check the type of conclusion: universal affirmative (requires first mood of first figure), universal negative (second mood of first, or first/second of second, or first of fourth), particular affirmative (third mood of first, or first/second/third of third, or second/third of fourth), particular negative (fourth mood of first, or third/fourth of second, or fourth/fifth/sixth of third, or fourth/fifth of fourth). Each mood must meet its conditions. Finally, the author remarks that conclusions proved by only one figure are harder than those provable by multiple figures, and universal affirmative is the hardest to prove because it relies on a single mood, whereas refutation can be done by showing the opposite in other figures.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Analyzing Terms and Attributes to Find the Middle Term
Author describes the process of analyzing terms and their attributes to find a middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to systematically analyze the terms of a desired conclusion and their attributes to identify a potential middle term.

Core Idea
Exhaustive analysis of attributes of both terms is the key to discovering the middle term that connects them.

Once you have a theoretical proposition, the first step is to set it up as a desired conclusion (matlub). Then you must examine each of its two terms carefully, listing all essential and accidental attributes that attach to them. This thorough investigation is what enables you to find a common term that can serve as the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Set up the desired conclusion
Write the proposition you want to prove as a subject-predicate statement (e.g., 'Nabidh is haram'). Identify the subject term (nabidh) and predicate term (haram).
2
Exhaustive analysis of each term
List all essential attributes (e.g., 'nabidh is an intoxicating beverage', 'haram is a legal ruling') and accidental attributes (e.g., 'nabidh is made from dates', 'haram is forbidden').
Do not arbitrarily stop; the more comprehensive, the better.
3
Examine attribution relations
Consider positive and negative relations between attributes and the terms. For example, 'intoxication' is an attribute of nabidh (positive), and 'intoxication' causes haram (positive).
4
Beware of useless investigations
Avoid investigating whether an attribute of an attribute is itself an attribute of the term, unless it is relevant. Also avoid seeking contradictions between the attributes of the two terms without a unified middle term.
The author warns against such futility.
5
Recognize the need for a single middle term
The middle term must be the same for both premises. Simply having two contradictory attributes (e.g., hot and cold) does not yield a syllogism unless there is a single linking term.
Example: 'Fire is hot and water is cold' does not give a syllogism because no single middle term links fire and water.

Chapter Five: On Acquiring Premises and How to Obtain Syllogisms for Desired Conclusions. Since we have finished explaining syllogisms and defining their types, it is fitting for us to complete the benefit by defining the methods of acquiring them in a general way, not in terms of being demonstrative, rhetorical, dialectical, or otherwise, as will come, for rarely does one benefit from knowing the reality of syllogism when wanting to use it without knowing the method of obtaining it, since he finds it obtained. If they are intuitive, there is no need to seek them, for what clarifies them is not clearer than them, and if some depend, it is only on the slightest recollection and reminder, as previously mentioned. Then examine what is attributed to each, and what each is attributed to, negatively or affirmatively, not minding whether the relation is natural like the relation of an accident to its subject or vice versa, or like the relation of an accident to an accident. According to the extent of investigation into these matters, the benefit increases. Beware of investigating here whether the attribute of an attribute is an attribute? Or whether the subject of a subject is a subject? For that is using what is already known. Or that you busy yourself with investigating what attaches to the two terms, unless their relation to them differs by necessity or possibility or other than that which we verified in the second figure, or that you busy yourself with what does not attach to them, due to its uselessness as has passed. Or you seek contradiction between the attributes of the two terms, or difference between them, to base on it the negation of one term from the other, as some might imagine, and it is as it is said: (Fire is hot and water is cold, or that this is an animal and this is inanimate) because the middle term must be one, and were it not that what is hot is not cold and what is cold is not hot, and that what is animal is not inanimate, and what is inanimate is not animal, otherwise the syllogism would not be valid. So the validity of the syllogism in such matters, if it exists, is only because what attaches to 'j', for example, is not an attribute of 'b', and vice versa, not because of what attaches to 'j' and 'b'. After finishing the investigation of these matters, it is obligatory to look at what is suitable for the desired conclusion among these propositions. Whatever we do not find suitable for the desired conclusion or for one of its two terms, then beware of occupying yourself with it, for it is useless. And what is suitable is either suitable for its entirety by being joined to it, or to its opposite, or to one of its two terms. If its suitability is for one of its two terms, then you must seek what is suitable for the other term. If you find, then either there is between the two opposites that are suitable for the two terms of the desired conclusion a common third term or not. If there is not, then no benefit from them in the desired conclusion. If there is, then if your desired conclusion is universal affirmative, and the common term is in the pattern of the first mood of the first figure, accompanied by its conditions, then your syllogism is complete, otherwise not. If your desired conclusion is universal negative, and the common term is in the pattern of the second mood of the first, or the first and second of the second figure, or the first of the fourth, each one being accompanied, then it is complete, otherwise not. If your desired conclusion is particular affirmative, and the middle term is in the pattern of the third mood of the first figure, or the first, second, and third of the third figure, or the second and third of the fourth figure, each one having its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete. If your desired conclusion is particular negative, and the middle term is in the pattern of the fourth mood of the first, or the third and fourth of the second, or the fourth, fifth, and sixth of the third, or the fourth and fifth of the fourth, and each one has its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete, otherwise not. At that point, know that what is clarified by only one figure is more difficult than what is clarified by several figures. Rather, what is clarified by one mood of a figure is more difficult than what is clarified by moods of that figure or another figure. The universal affirmative is not clarified except by the first mood of the first figure, so proving it is more difficult than others. Rather, proving it is more difficult than disproving it, because proving depends on one way, while disproving may be by demonstrating the opposite, and that is in the figures.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage presents the method for acquiring premises to construct a syllogism for a desired conclusion in logic. The author begins by distinguishing between intuitive (badihi) and theoretical (nazari) tasdiqi meanings. Intuitive propositions require no search, while theoretical ones need a systematic procedure. The method: first, set up the desired conclusion (matlub) and analyze each of its two terms (haddain) thoroughly, examining their essential and accidental attributes exhaustively. Then examine the relations of attribution (positive or negative) between these attributes and the terms. The author warns against useless investigations, such as whether an attribute of an attribute is itself an attribute, or seeking contradiction or difference between the attributes of the two terms without a proper middle term. He illustrates with an example: 'Fire is hot and water is cold' or 'This is an animal and this is inanimate'—the middle term must be one, and the negation of one attribute from the other is not enough; the syllogism works only because the attributes of 'j' and 'b' are mutually exclusive. After this preparatory analysis, the next step is to find propositions that are suitable (munasib) for the desired conclusion or its terms. If a proposition suits one term, seek one that suits the other. If there is common term between them, then check the type of conclusion: universal affirmative (requires first mood of first figure), universal negative (second mood of first, or first/second of second, or first of fourth), particular affirmative (third mood of first, or first/second/third of third, or second/third of fourth), particular negative (fourth mood of first, or third/fourth of second, or fourth/fifth/sixth of third, or fourth/fifth of fourth). Each mood must meet its conditions. Finally, the author remarks that conclusions proved by only one figure are harder than those provable by multiple figures, and universal affirmative is the hardest to prove because it relies on a single mood, whereas refutation can be done by showing the opposite in other figures.

Selecting the Correct Figure and Mood for Your Conclusion
Author enumerates which figures and moods work for each type of conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map a desired conclusion to the specific figure and mood required for a valid syllogism, based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
Each type of conclusion (universal affirmative, etc.) can only be proven through specific figure-mood combinations.

After you have identified a common middle term, you must determine whether the pattern of the middle term's position matches a valid mood in the appropriate figure. The author provides a detailed mapping for each type of conclusion: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. This ensures that your syllogism is formally valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of your desired conclusion
Determine if it is universal affirmative (e.g., 'All A are B'), universal negative (No A are B), particular affirmative (Some A are B), or particular negative (Some A are not B).
2
For universal affirmative: use only first figure, first mood
The middle term must be subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor, with universal affirmative propositions. Example: 'All intoxicants are haram; all nabidh are intoxicants; so all nabidh are haram.'
3
For universal negative: check allowed moods
Options: second mood of first figure (Celarent), first and second of second figure (Cesare and Camestres), first of fourth figure. Each has specific proposition types.
You must also ensure the middle term position matches the description in the passage.
4
For particular affirmative: check allowed moods
Options: third mood of first figure (Darii), first, second, third of third figure (Disamis, Datisi, etc.?), second and third of fourth figure.
5
For particular negative: check allowed moods
Options: fourth mood of first figure (Ferio), third and fourth of second figure (Festino, Baroco), fourth, fifth, sixth of third figure, fourth and fifth of fourth figure.

وإذ قد فرغنا من بيان الأقيسة، وتعريف أصنافها، فجدير بنا تكميل الانتفاع بتعريف طرق اكتسابها على وجه كلي لا من حيث هو برهاني، أو خطابي، أو جدلي، أو غير ذلك كما يأتي، إذ قل ما ينتفع بمعرفة حقيقة القياس عند إرادة استعماله من لا يعرف طريق تحصيله، إذ يجده محصلاً.
فنقول: المعاني التصديقية إما بديهية أو نظرية فإن كانت بديهية فلا حاجة إلى طلبها، فإن ما يتبين به ليس بأبين منها، وافتقار بعضها إن كان فليس إلا إلى أدنى تذكر وتنبيه كما سبق.
وإن كانت نظرية فكيفية طريق اكتساب كل مطلوب منها أن تضع أولاً إحدى المطلوب ثم تنظر في حد كل واحد واحد من الحدّين، وما يلحق بكل واحد منهما من الذاتيات والعرضيات، وذاتيات الذاتيات والعرضيات وعرضيات كل واحد منها على جهة الاستقصاء، ثم تنظر في ما ينسب إلى كل واحد، وما ينسب هو إليه سلباً أو إيجاباً، ولا تبال كانت النسبة طبيعية كنسبة العارض إلى موضوعه أو بالعكس، أو كنسبة عارض إلى عارض، وعلى حسب زيادة البحث عن هذه الأمور تكون زيادة الانتفاع، وإياك والبحث هاهنا عن لاحق اللاحق هل هو لاحق؟ أو عن ملحوق الملحوق هل هو ملحوق؟ إذ هو استعمال بما هو معلوم، أو أن تشتغل بالبحث عما يلحق الحدّين، إلا أن تختلف نسبته إليهما بالضرورة والإمكان أو غير ذلك مما حققناه في الشكل الثاني أو أن تشتغل بما لا يلحق بهما، لعدم جدواه كما مضى، أو تطلب التضاد بين لواحق الحدين، أو التغاير بينهما، لتبني عليه سلب أحد الحدين عن الآخر، كما قد يتخيل متخيل، وهو كما يقال: (النار حارة والماء بارد أو أن هذا حيوان وهذا (جماد) إذ الحد الأوسط يجب أن يكون واحداً، ولولا أن ما هو حار ليس ببارد وما هو بارد ليس بحار وأن ما هو حيوان ليس بجماد) و(ما هو جماد ليس بحيوان وإلا لما لزم القياس، فانعقاد القياس في مثل هذه الأمور إن كان، فليس إلا لأن ما لحق "ج" مثلاً غير لاحق لـ "ب" وبالعكس، لا لما لحق "ج" و"ب".

وعند ذلك فتعلم أن ما يتبين من المطالب بشكل واحد فقط أصعب مما يتبين بأشكال عدة، بل وما يتبين بضرب واحد من الشكل فهو أصعب مما يتبين بضروب من اقترانات ذلك الشكل أو شكل غيره، والكلي الموجب لا يتبين لغير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول، فإثباته أعسر من غيره، بل وإثباته أعسر من إبطاله؛ لتوقف الإثبات على وجه واحد والإبطال فقد يكون ببيان النقيض، وذلك في الأشكال
Chapter Five: On Acquiring Premises and How to Obtain Syllogisms for Desired Conclusions. Since we have finished explaining syllogisms and defining their types, it is fitting for us to complete the benefit by defining the methods of acquiring them in a general way, not in terms of being demonstrative, rhetorical, dialectical, or otherwise, as will come, for rarely does one benefit from knowing the reality of syllogism when wanting to use it without knowing the method of obtaining it, since he finds it obtained. We say: Tasdiqi meanings are either intuitive or theoretical. If they are intuitive, there is no need to seek them, for what clarifies them is not clearer than them, and if some depend, it is only on the slightest recollection and reminder, as previously mentioned. If they are theoretical, then the method of acquiring each desired conclusion from them is that you first set up one of the desired conclusions, then examine the definition of each one of the two terms, and what attaches to each of them from essential and accidental attributes, and the essentials of essentials and accidents, and the accidents of each of them exhaustively. Then examine what is attributed to each, and what each is attributed to, negatively or affirmatively, not minding whether the relation is natural like the relation of an accident to its subject or vice versa, or like the relation of an accident to an accident. According to the extent of investigation into these matters, the benefit increases. Beware of investigating here whether the attribute of an attribute is an attribute? Or whether the subject of a subject is a subject? For that is using what is already known. Or that you busy yourself with investigating what attaches to the two terms, unless their relation to them differs by necessity or possibility or other than that which we verified in the second figure, or that you busy yourself with what does not attach to them, due to its uselessness as has passed. Or you seek contradiction between the attributes of the two terms, or difference between them, to base on it the negation of one term from the other, as some might imagine, and it is as it is said: (Fire is hot and water is cold, or that this is an animal and this is inanimate) because the middle term must be one, and were it not that what is hot is not cold and what is cold is not hot, and that what is animal is not inanimate, and what is inanimate is not animal, otherwise the syllogism would not be valid. So the validity of the syllogism in such matters, if it exists, is only because what attaches to 'j', for example, is not an attribute of 'b', and vice versa, not because of what attaches to 'j' and 'b'. After finishing the investigation of these matters, it is obligatory to look at what is suitable for the desired conclusion among these propositions. Whatever we do not find suitable for the desired conclusion or for one of its two terms, then beware of occupying yourself with it, for it is useless. And what is suitable is either suitable for its entirety by being joined to it, or to its opposite, or to one of its two terms. If its suitability is for one of its two terms, then you must seek what is suitable for the other term. If you find, then either there is between the two opposites that are suitable for the two terms of the desired conclusion a common third term or not. If there is not, then no benefit from them in the desired conclusion. If there is, then if your desired conclusion is universal affirmative, and the common term is in the pattern of the first mood of the first figure, accompanied by its conditions, then your syllogism is complete, otherwise not. If your desired conclusion is universal negative, and the common term is in the pattern of the second mood of the first, or the first and second of the second figure, or the first of the fourth, each one being accompanied, then it is complete, otherwise not. If your desired conclusion is particular affirmative, and the middle term is in the pattern of the third mood of the first figure, or the first, second, and third of the third figure, or the second and third of the fourth figure, each one having its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete. If your desired conclusion is particular negative, and the middle term is in the pattern of the fourth mood of the first, or the third and fourth of the second, or the fourth, fifth, and sixth of the third, or the fourth and fifth of the fourth, and each one has its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete, otherwise not. At that point, know that what is clarified by only one figure is more difficult than what is clarified by several figures. Rather, what is clarified by one mood of a figure is more difficult than what is clarified by moods of that figure or another figure. The universal affirmative is not clarified except by the first mood of the first figure, so proving it is more difficult than others. Rather, proving it is more difficult than disproving it, because proving depends on one way, while disproving may be by demonstrating the opposite, and that is in the figures.

Commentary
This passage presents the method for acquiring premises to construct a syllogism for a desired conclusion in logic. The Syllogism
intermediate

Method of Finding Appropriate Premises for the Sought Conclusion
Author introduces the method for selecting premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a premise is appropriate for a given sought conclusion and apply the method of seeking premises for each limit.

Core Idea
The middle term must connect the sought conclusion's limits appropriately.

To construct a valid syllogism, you must first find premises relevant to the sought conclusion or its limits. This lesson introduces the method for doing so, as outlined by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sought conclusion and its limits
Find the maṭlūb (conclusion to prove) and its two ḥaddān (subject and predicate). For example, 'every intoxicant is forbidden' has limits 'intoxicant' and 'forbidden'.
2
Determine appropriateness of a premise
A premise is appropriate if it relates to the whole conclusion, its contradictory, or one of its limits. If not, it is useless.
3
If appropriate for one limit, seek the other
If a premise matches one limit, search for another premise appropriate for the second limit. Without both, the syllogism fails.
4
Check for a common limit
Between the two premises that match the limits, see if they share a common term. If not, they are useless for proving the sought conclusion.

Continuation of the fourth chapter: On defining the forms of arguments. And (that which is inanimate is not an animal, otherwise the syllogism would not be necessary, so the constitution of a syllogism in such matters, if it occurs, is only because what attaches to 'j' for example does not attach to 'b' and vice versa, not because of what attaches to 'j' and 'b'. And after finishing the investigation of these matters, it is obligatory to look into what is appropriate for the sought conclusion from among these propositions. If its appropriateness is for one of its two limits, then you must seek what is appropriate for the other limit. If you find it, then either there is between the two contradictories that are appropriate for the two limits of the sought conclusion a commonality in another limit, or there is not. If there is not, then there is no benefit from them in the sought conclusion. And if there is, then if your sought conclusion is universal affirmative and the common limit is in the pattern of the first mood of the first figure, connected with its conditions, then your syllogism is complete, otherwise not. Commentary
This passage provides a detailed methodology for constructing syllogisms tailored to the type of conclusion desired. The author begins with an illustrative example: an inanimate thing is not an animal, which clarifies that the middle term must be such that the predicate attaches to it in a specific way. Then he outlines a step-by-step process: after examining the propositions, one must find a premise appropriate for the sought conclusion or its limits. If it is appropriate for one limit, one must seek the appropriate premise for the other limit. If there is a common term between the two contradictory premises, then depending on the conclusion type, specific moods are valid. For a universal affirmative conclusion, only the first mood of the first figure works. For universal negative, the second of the first figure, the first and second of the second, or the first of the fourth are valid. For particular affirmative, the third of the first, the first three of the third, or the second and third of the fourth are valid. For particular negative, the fourth of the first, the third and fourth of the second, the fourth to sixth of the third, or the fourth and fifth of the fourth are valid. Each mood must have its conditions fulfilled. This is a technical reference for identifying the correct syllogistic form for any given categorical proposition.

Key Terms
مطلوب
حد
شكل
اقتران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a premise is appropriate for one limit of the sought conclusion, what must you do next?
A) Conclude the syllogism immediately.
B) Seek another premise appropriate for the other limit.
C) Check if the premise is universal affirmative.
D) Discard the premise as useless.

Key Takeaway
The first step in constructing a syllogism is to find premises that are appropriate for the sought conclusion or its limits, and then ensure they share a common term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Valid Moods for Universal Affirmative Conclusions
Author lists valid mood for universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid mood for proving a universal affirmative conclusion (kullī mūjib) and apply it to an Islamic example.

Core Idea
Only the first mood of the first figure proves a universal affirmative.

When your sought conclusion is universal affirmative, the middle term must be arranged in the pattern of the first mood of the first figure, with all conditions satisfied. This lesson examines that mood and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Ensure the sought conclusion is universal affirmative: 'Every A is B.'
2
Recall valid mood
The author states only the first mood of the first figure works: middle term is subject in major and predicate in minor.
3
Construct the syllogism
Example: Major: Every intoxicant is forbidden (universal affirmative). Minor: Every nabidh is intoxicant (universal affirmative). Conclusion: Every nabidh is forbidden.
Check that the conditions (e.g., affirmative premises, universal minor) are met.
4
Verify validity
In first figure first mood (Barbara), the syllogism is valid if both premises are universal affirmative and the middle term is distributed appropriately.

تتمة المقالة الرابعة في تعريف صور الحجج
و(ما هو جماد ليس بحيوان وإلا لما لزم القياس، فانعقاد القياس في مثل هذه الأمور إن كان، فليس إلا لأن ما لحق "ج" مثلاً غير لاحق لـ "ب" وبالعكس، لا لما لحق "ج" و"ب".
وبعد الفراغ عن البحث في هذه الأمور فالواجب أن ننظر في ما يناسب المطلوب من هذه القضايا فما لم نجده مناسباً للمطلوب أو لأحـد حـديـه فإياك والاشتغال به؛ إذ هو عديم الجدوى، وما يناسب فإما أن يكون مناسباً لكليته بأن يكون منضماً له، أو لنقيضه، أو لأحد حديه، فإن كانت مناسبته لأحد حديه فعليك بطلب ما يناسب الحد الآخر، فإن وجدت فإما أن يكون بين النقيضين المناسبتين لحدي المطلوب اشتراك في حد آخر أو ليس فإن كان ليس فلا انتفاع بهما في المطلوب، وإن كان فإن كان مطلوبك كلياً موجباً وكان الحد المشترك على نمط الأول من الشكل الأول مقترناً بشرائط فقد تم قياسك وإلا فلا وإن كان مطلوبك كلياً سالباً وكان الحد المشترك على نمط الاقتران الثاني من الأول، أو الأول والثاني من الشكل الثاني أو الأول من الرابع، وكل واحد مقترن فقد تم وإلا فلا، وإن كان مطلوبك جزئياً موجباً وكان الحد الأوسط على نمط الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول، أو الأول والثاني والثالث من الشكل الثالث أو الثاني والثالث من الشكل الرابع، وكل واحد قد اقترنت به شرائطه فقد تم القياس أيضاً، وإن كان مطلوبك جزئياً سالباً وكان الحد الأوسط على نمط الاقتران الرابع من الأول، أو الثالث والرابع من الشكل الثاني أو الرابع والخامس والسادس من الثالث، أو الرابع والخامس من الرابع، وكان كل واحد قد اقترنت به شرائطه فقد تم القياس أيضاً وإلا فلا
Continuation of the fourth chapter: On defining the forms of arguments. And (that which is inanimate is not an animal, otherwise the syllogism would not be necessary, so the constitution of a syllogism in such matters, if it occurs, is only because what attaches to 'j' for example does not attach to 'b' and vice versa, not because of what attaches to 'j' and 'b'. And after finishing the investigation of these matters, it is obligatory to look into what is appropriate for the sought conclusion from among these propositions. So if we do not find it appropriate for the sought conclusion or for one of its two limits, then avoid occupying yourself with it, for it is useless. And what is appropriate, then either it is appropriate for the whole, i.e., being conjoined with it, or for its contradictory, or for one of its two limits. If its appropriateness is for one of its two limits, then you must seek what is appropriate for the other limit. If you find it, then either there is between the two contradictories that are appropriate for the two limits of the sought conclusion a commonality in another limit, or there is not. If there is not, then there is no benefit from them in the sought conclusion. And if there is, then if your sought conclusion is universal affirmative and the common limit is in the pattern of the first mood of the first figure, connected with its conditions, then your syllogism is complete, otherwise not. And if your sought conclusion is universal negative and the common limit is in the pattern of the second mood of the first figure, or the first and second of the second figure, or the first of the fourth figure, each one connected [with conditions], then it is complete, otherwise not. And if your sought conclusion is particular affirmative and the middle term is in the pattern of the third mood of the first figure, or the first, second, and third of the third figure, or the second and third of the fourth figure, and each one has its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete. And if your sought conclusion is particular negative and the middle term is in the pattern of the fourth mood of the first figure, or the third and fourth of the second figure, or the fourth, fifth, and sixth of the third figure, or the fourth and fifth of the fourth figure, and each one has its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete, otherwise not.

Valid Moods for Universal Negative Conclusions
Author lists valid moods for universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid moods for proving a universal negative conclusion (kullī sālib) and construct an example.

Core Idea
Universal negative conclusions are proven by specific moods in the first, second, or fourth figures.

When your sought conclusion is universal negative, the author lists several valid moods: the second mood of the first figure, the first and second moods of the second figure, or the first mood of the fourth figure. This lesson explores these options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Ensure the sought conclusion is universal negative: 'No A is B.'
2
Recall valid moods
The author enumerates: second of first figure, first and second of second figure, or first of fourth figure.
3
Select and construct a valid syllogism
Example using first figure second mood (Celarent): Major: No intoxicant is permissible (universal negative). Minor: Every khamr is intoxicant (universal affirmative). Conclusion: No khamr is permissible.
Check conditions: major universal negative, minor universal affirmative, middle term distributed in major.
4
Verify validity
In first figure second mood, the syllogism is valid if major is universal negative and minor is universal affirmative, with middle term subject in major and predicate in minor.

وبعد الفراغ عن البحث في هذه الأمور فالواجب أن ننظر في ما يناسب المطلوب من هذه القضايا فما لم نجده مناسباً للمطلوب أو لأحـد حـديـه فإياك والاشتغال به؛ إذ هو عديم الجدوى، وما يناسب فإما أن يكون مناسباً لكليته بأن يكون منضماً له، أو لنقيضه، أو لأحد حديه، فإن كانت مناسبته لأحد حديه فعليك بطلب ما يناسب الحد الآخر، فإن وجدت فإما أن يكون بين النقيضين المناسبتين لحدي المطلوب اشتراك في حد آخر أو ليس فإن كان ليس فلا انتفاع بهما في المطلوب، وإن كان فإن كان مطلوبك كلياً موجباً وكان الحد المشترك على نمط الأول من الشكل الأول مقترناً بشرائط فقد تم قياسك وإلا فلا وإن كان مطلوبك كلياً سالباً وكان الحد المشترك على نمط الاقتران الثاني من الأول، أو الأول والثاني من الشكل الثاني أو الأول من الرابع، وكل واحد مقترن فقد تم وإلا فلا، وإن كان مطلوبك جزئياً موجباً وكان الحد الأوسط على نمط الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول، أو الأول والثاني والثالث من الشكل الثالث أو الثاني والثالث من الشكل الرابع، وكل واحد قد اقترنت به شرائطه فقد تم القياس أيضاً، وإن كان مطلوبك جزئياً سالباً وكان الحد الأوسط على نمط الاقتران الرابع من الأول، أو الثالث والرابع من الشكل الثاني أو الرابع والخامس والسادس من الثالث، أو الرابع والخامس من الرابع، وكان كل واحد قد اقترنت به شرائطه فقد تم القياس أيضاً وإلا فلا
Continuation of the fourth chapter: On defining the forms of arguments. And (that which is inanimate is not an animal, otherwise the syllogism would not be necessary, so the constitution of a syllogism in such matters, if it occurs, is only because what attaches to 'j' for example does not attach to 'b' and vice versa, not because of what attaches to 'j' and 'b'. And after finishing the investigation of these matters, it is obligatory to look into what is appropriate for the sought conclusion from among these propositions. So if we do not find it appropriate for the sought conclusion or for one of its two limits, then avoid occupying yourself with it, for it is useless. And what is appropriate, then either it is appropriate for the whole, i.e., being conjoined with it, or for its contradictory, or for one of its two limits. If its appropriateness is for one of its two limits, then you must seek what is appropriate for the other limit. If you find it, then either there is between the two contradictories that are appropriate for the two limits of the sought conclusion a commonality in another limit, or there is not. If there is not, then there is no benefit from them in the sought conclusion. And if there is, then if your sought conclusion is universal affirmative and the common limit is in the pattern of the first mood of the first figure, connected with its conditions, then your syllogism is complete, otherwise not. And if your sought conclusion is universal negative and the common limit is in the pattern of the second mood of the first figure, or the first and second of the second figure, or the first of the fourth figure, each one connected [with conditions], then it is complete, otherwise not. And if your sought conclusion is particular affirmative and the middle term is in the pattern of the third mood of the first figure, or the first, second, and third of the third figure, or the second and third of the fourth figure, and each one has its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete. And if your sought conclusion is particular negative and the middle term is in the pattern of the fourth mood of the first figure, or the third and fourth of the second figure, or the fourth, fifth, and sixth of the third figure, or the fourth and fifth of the fourth figure, and each one has its conditions attached, then the syllogism is also complete, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed methodology for constructing syllogisms tailored to the type of conclusion desired. The author begins with an illustrative example: an inanimate thing is not an animal, which clarifies that the middle term must be such that the predicate attaches to it in a specific way. Then he outlines a step-by-step process: after examining the propositions, one must find a premise appropriate for the sought conclusion or its limits. If it is appropriate for one limit, one must seek the appropriate premise for the other limit. If there is a common term between the two contradictory premises, then depending on the conclusion type, specific moods are valid. For a universal affirmative conclusion, only the first mood of the first figure works. For universal negative, the second of the first figure, the first and second of the second, or the first of the fourth are valid. For particular affirmative, the third of the first, the first three of the third, or the second and third of the fourth are valid. For particular negative, the fourth of the first, the third and fourth of the second, the fourth to sixth of the third, or the fourth and fifth of the fourth are valid. Each mood must have its conditions fulfilled. This is a technical reference for identifying the correct syllogistic form for any given categorical proposition.
