Proving Universal Propositions: Why the Universal Affirmative Is Hardest
Author compares difficulty of proving universal and particular propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the difficulty of proving universal affirmative and universal negative propositions based on the number of available syllogistic moods and figures.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative can only be proven by one mood; universal negative by four.

In this passage, the author establishes an epistemological hierarchy: the fewer ways a proposition can be proven, the harder its proof. We will apply this principle to universal affirmative and universal negative categorical propositions, showing why the universal affirmative is the most difficult to establish.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
The author states that what can be proven by only one mode is more difficult than what can be proven by several modes. This is the key insight for ranking propositions.
2
Identify the universal affirmative's proof methods
The universal affirmative (e.g., 'All humans are mortal') can only be proven by the first mood of the first figure. Count: only one mood.
This mood is Barbara in Latin terms.
3
Identify the universal negative's proof methods
The universal negative (e.g., 'No human is immortal') can be proven by four moods across three figures: second mood of first figure, first and second of second figure, and first of fourth figure.
These correspond to Celarent, Cesare, Camestres, and Camenes.
4
Compare difficulty
Since the universal affirmative has fewer proof methods (1 vs 4), it is harder to prove than the universal negative. This difference illustrates the author's hierarchy.
The author explicitly says the universal affirmative's proof is 'harder than others'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند ذلك فتعلم أن ما يتبين من المطالب بشكل واحد فقط أصعب مما يتبين بأشكال عدة، بل وما يتبين بضرب واحد من الشكل فهو أصعب مما يتبين بضروب من اقترانات ذلك الشكل أو شكل غيره، والكلي الموجب لا يتبين لغير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول، فإثباته أعسر من غيره، بل وإثباته أعسر من إبطاله؛ لتوقف الإثبات على وجه واحد والإبطال فقد يكون ببيان النقيض، وذلك في الأشكال الأربعة باقترانات ثمانية على ما عرف، وقد يكون ذلك ببيان الضد وبيانه من الأشكال الثلاثة باقترانات أربعة كما سلف وما توقف على وجه واحد فأصعب مما يتبين بأحد عشر وجهاً، ثم يليه في الصعوبة الكلي السالب، فإن إثباته لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول والأول والثاني من الثاني، والأول من الرابع، فبيانه باقترانات أربعة من الأشكال الثلاثة.
ومع
والجزئي إيجاباً وسلباً، فبيانه من الأشكال الأربعة، وباقترانات تكثر من أربعة، ذلك فإبطاله أسهل من إثباته؛ إذ الإبطال قد يكون ببيان ضده، وذلك باقتران واحد من شكل واحد وبيان نقيضه، وهو قد يكون باقترانات سبعة من الأول والثالث والرابع ؛ ولذلك كان مما يلي الكلي السالب في الصعوبة، إذ بيان الجزئي السالب قد يكون باقترانات ثمانية من الأشكال الأربعة.
ثم الجزئي السالب ولما كان الجزئي إيجاباً وسلباً مما لا ضد له، وإنما إبطاله ببیان نقيضه، ونقيض الجزئي الموجب كلي سالب والجزئي السالب كليّ موجب وقد بان أن طرق إثبات الجزئي أكثر من الكلي؛ [لذا] كان إبطال الجزئي إيجاباً وسلباً أصعب من إثباته.
فعلى هذا كل ما كان أصعب إثباتاً من غيره فإبطاله أسهل من إبطال ذلك الغير
وما كان أسهل إبطالاً من إبطال غيره فثبوته أصعب من إثبات ذلك الغير.
وأما إن كانت مناسبته لحدي المطلوب أو نقيضه فالقضية شرطية، فعليك بمراعاة ما يصح استثناؤه منها من العين أو النقيض ولا تبال كانت متصلة أو منفصلة، وإذ ذاك فيكون قياسك قد تم من الاستثنائيات، على ما أشرنا إليه في تعرف القياسين - أعني الاقتراني والاستثنائي - يكون تعرف قياس الخلف، لتركبه منها، ولأنه قد يمكن رده إلى المستقيم، مع بقاء حدوده بأعيانها، وهذا (...) فكره من الإشارة إلى اكتساب أقيسة المطلوب.
And at that point, know that what is made evident from among the sought conclusions by only one mode is more difficult than what is made evident by several modes; rather, what is made evident by one species of the figure is more difficult than what is made evident by species of moods of that figure or another figure. And the universal affirmative is not made evident except by the first mood of the first figure, so its proof is more difficult than others; indeed, its proof is more difficult than its refutation, because proof depends on one way, while refutation may be by proving the contradictory, and that in the four figures with eight moods as known, and it may be by proving the contrary, and its proof from the three figures with four moods as previously mentioned. And what depends on one way is more difficult than what is made evident by eleven ways. Then next in difficulty is the universal negative, for its proof is only from the second mood of the first figure and the first and second of the second and the first of the fourth, so its demonstration is by four moods from the three figures. And the particular, both affirmative and negative, its demonstration is from the four figures, and by moods that are more than four, so its refutation is easier than its proof; for refutation may be by proving its contrary, and that by one mood from one figure, and by proving its contradictory, and that may be by seven moods from the first, third, and fourth. Therefore it follows the universal negative in difficulty, since the demonstration of the particular negative may be by eight moods from the four figures. Then the particular negative—and since the particular affirmative and negative have no contrary, but only refutation by proving their contradictory, and the contradictory of the particular affirmative is universal negative and of the particular negative is universal affirmative, and it has become clear that the ways of proving the particular are more than those of the universal; therefore refutation of the particular, affirmative and negative, is more difficult than its proof. Based on this, whatever is harder to prove than another, its refutation is easier than the refutation of that other, and whatever is easier to refute than another, its proof is harder than the proof of that other. And if its appropriateness is for the two limits of the sought conclusion or its contradictory, then the premise is conditional, so you must observe what can be deduced from it, either the exact thing or its contradictory, and do not mind whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive. Then at that point your syllogism is complete from conditional syllogisms, according to what we indicated in the knowledge of the two types of syllogisms—I mean the conjunctive and the conditional—and so the knowledge of the syllogism of reductio, because it is composed of them, and because it can be reduced to the direct, while its terms remain themselves. And this (...) is the thought of indicating the acquisition of the syllogisms of the sought conclusion.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological hierarchy of proving categorical propositions. The author states that a proposition proven by only one method is harder than one proven by several. The universal affirmative is the hardest because it can only be proven by the first mood of the first figure; thus its proof is more difficult than its refutation, since refutation can be achieved by proving the contradictory (which has many ways) or the contrary. Next, the universal negative is easier, as it has four possible moods from three figures. Particular propositions (affirmative and negative) have many proofs (more than four moods across all figures), so their refutation is easier than their proof because refutation can be done by proving the contrary (one mood) or the contradictory (seven moods). However, the particular negative is special: since particular propositions have no contrary, their refutation requires proving the contradictory. The contradictory of a particular affirmative is universal negative (which is harder to prove) and of a particular negative is universal affirmative (the hardest). Hence, for particular propositions, refutation is actually more difficult than proof. The general rule is: what is harder to prove is easier to refute, and vice versa. Finally, if the premise is appropriate for the limits or their contradictory, it becomes a conditional proposition (conjunctive or disjunctive). In conditional syllogisms, one deduces either the exact consequent or its contradictory. This leads to the reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf), which can be reduced to a direct syllogism.

The fewer syllogistic moods that can prove a proposition, the harder it is to demonstrate.

Particular Propositions: Why Refutation Is Harder Than Proof
Author explains refutation difficulty for particular propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether refutation or proof is easier for particular affirmative and particular negative propositions based on their logical relationships.

Core Idea
For particular propositions, refutation is harder because it requires proving a universal contradictory.

The author extends his analysis to particular propositions, which have many proof methods. Surprisingly, their refutation is more difficult than their proof because they have no contraries, and their contradictories are universal propositions with fewer proof methods. We will unpack this logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the properties of particular propositions
Particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some humans are mortal') and particular negative (e.g., 'Some humans are not mortal') have no contraries. Their refutation can only be done by proving their contradictories.
A contrary (ضد) is a proposition that cannot both be true but may both be false. Particulars lack this.
2
Identify the contradictories
The contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative; the contradictory of a particular negative is a universal affirmative. For example, the contradictory of 'Some humans are mortal' is 'No humans are mortal'.
These contradictories follow the square of opposition.
3
Compare proof methods for original vs contradictory
The author states that the ways of proving the particular are more than those of the universal. In fact, particular propositions can be proven by many moods (more than four across all figures). Universal affirmatives have only one mood, universal negatives have four. So proving the universal contradictory is harder than proving the original particular.
Thus, refutation (proving the contradictory) is harder than proof (proving the original).
4
Apply the inverse rule
The author's general rule: 'Whatever is harder to prove than another, its refutation is easier than the refutation of that other.' But here, for particular propositions, proof is easier, so refutation is harder. This is consistent.
This completes the hierarchy.

Difficulty of Proving Universal vs Particular Propositions
Author compares proving/refuting universal and particular propositions via syllogistic forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the number of valid syllogistic moods required to prove each of the four categorical propositions.

Core Idea
Universal affirmatives require eleven moods to prove; particular negatives require eight; universal negatives four; particulars are easier overall.

This passage ranks the four categorical proposition types by how many distinct valid syllogistic moods (iqtirānāt) are needed to demonstrate them. This ranking reflects the logical structure of proof within the four figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposition types
The passage discusses universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Recognize them in Arabic: كلي موجب, كلي سالب, جزئي موجب, جزئي سالب.
2
Find the number of moods for each
Universal affirmative requires up to eleven faces. Universal negative requires four specific moods: second of first figure, first and second of second, first of fourth. Particular negative requires eight moods. Particular affirmative is proven from all four figures with many combinations.
The author does not give an exact number for particular affirmative but implies many.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the numbers
The specific moods are listed for universal negative: it can only be proven via those four combinations. The universal affirmative has the most because it is the strongest claim.
4
Apply this to a real example from fiqh
For instance, proving that 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative) takes more logical work than proving that 'No intoxicant is permissible' (universal negative).
This helps anticipate the evidential burden for different types of legal claims.

الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج الأربعة باقترانات ثمانية على ما عرف، وقد يكون ذلك ببيان الضد وبيانه من الأشكال الثلاثة باقترانات أربعة كما سلف وما توقف على وجه واحد فأصعب مما يتبين بأحد عشر وجهاً، ثم يليه في الصعوبة الكلي السالب، فإن إثباته لا يكون من غير الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول والأول والثاني من الثاني، والأول من الرابع، فبيانه باقترانات أربعة من الأشكال الثلاثة. ومع الجزئي إيجاباً وسلباً، فبيانه من الأشكال الأربعة، وباقترانات تكثر من أربعة، ذلك فإبطاله أسهل من إثباته؛ إذ الإبطال قد يكون ببيان ضده، وذلك باقتران واحد من شكل واحد وبيان نقيضه، وهو قد يكون باقترانات سبعة من الأول والثالث والرابع ؛ ولذلك كان مما يلي الكلي السالب في الصعوبة، إذ بيان الجزئي السالب قد يكون باقترانات ثمانية من الأشكال الأربعة. ثم الجزئي السالب ولما كان الجزئي إيجاباً وسلباً مما لا ضد له، وإنما إبطاله ببیان نقيضه، ونقيض الجزئي الموجب كلي سالب والجزئي السالب كليّ موجب وقد بان أن طرق إثبات الجزئي أكثر من الكلي؛ [لذا] كان إبطال الجزئي إيجاباً وسلباً أصعب من إثباته. فعلى هذا كل ما كان أصعب إثباتاً من غيره فإبطاله أسهل من إبطال ذلك الغير وما كان أسهل إبطالاً من إبطال غيره فثبوته أصعب من إثبات ذلك الغير. وأما إن كانت مناسبته لحدي المطلوب أو نقيضه فالقضية شرطية، فعليك بمراعاة ما يصح استثناؤه منها من العين أو النقيض ولا تبال كانت متصلة أو منفصلة، وإذ ذاك فيكون قياسك قد تم من الاستثنائيات، على ما أشرنا إليه في تعرف القياسين - أعني الاقتراني والاستثنائي - يكون تعرف قياس الخلف، لتركبه منها، ولأنه قد يمكن رده إلى المستقيم، مع بقاء حدوده بأعيانها، وهذا (...) فكره من الإشارة إلى اكتساب أقيسة المطلوب.
The eighth section on considering beneficial matters in the forms of arguments. The four [propositions] with eight combinations as known, and that may be by stating the opposite and its statement from the three figures with four combinations as previously, and what depends on one face is harder than what becomes clear by eleven faces. Then next in difficulty is the universal negative, for its proof is not except from the second combination of the first figure and the first and second of the second, and the first of the fourth; so its demonstration is by four combinations from the three figures. And with the particular, affirmative and negative, its demonstration is from the four figures, and by combinations numbering from four, so its refutation is easier than its proof; for refutation may be by stating its opposite, and that is by one combination from one figure, and by stating its contradictory, and it may be by seven combinations from the first, third, and fourth; therefore it was among those next to the universal negative in difficulty, for the demonstration of the particular negative may be by eight combinations from the four figures. Then the particular negative: and since the particular, affirmative and negative, has no opposite, and its refutation is only by stating its contradictory, and the contradictory of the particular affirmative is a universal negative, and the particular negative is a universal affirmative, and it has become clear that the ways of proving the particular are more than the universal; therefore refuting the particular, affirmative and negative, is harder than proving it. On this basis, whatever is harder to prove than another, its refutation is easier than the refutation of that other; and whatever is easier to refute than another's refutation, its proof is harder than the proof of that other. And as for if its suitability is to the two limits of the sought or its contradictory, then the proposition is conditional; upon you to observe what may be extracted from it of the same or the contradictory, and do not mind whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, and then your syllogism will have been completed from the conditional [syllogisms], according to what we indicated in the knowledge of the two syllogisms – I mean the conjunctive and the conditional – [and] the knowledge of the syllogism of reduction [reductio] will be, due to its composition from them, and because it can be reduced to the straight, with its limits remaining the same, and this (...) is his thought from the indication to acquiring syllogisms of the sought.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is from the eighth section of the work, discussing the relative ease or difficulty of proving and refuting different types of categorical propositions using the four figures of the syllogism. The author begins by noting that the four propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) can be proven using eight combinations (valid moods) from the four figures, as is known. However, some propositions are easier or harder to demonstrate. The universal affirmative is the hardest to prove, requiring up to eleven faces (i.e., eleven distinct valid moods or argument forms). Next in difficulty is the universal negative, which can only be proven using four specific combinations: the second mood of the first figure, the first and second moods of the second figure, and the first mood of the fourth figure. For particular propositions (both affirmative and negative), demonstration is possible from all four figures with many combinations, but refutation is easier than proof because refutation can be done by stating the opposite (using only one combination from one figure) or by stating the contradictory (using seven combinations from the first, third, and fourth figures). The particular negative is next in difficulty after the universal negative. The author then explains that particular propositions have no opposite, so their refutation is only by stating the contradictory. Since the contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative, and of a particular negative is a universal affirmative, and since there are more ways to prove particulars than universals, refuting particulars is harder than proving them. The author summarizes a general rule: the harder a proposition is to prove, the easier it is to refute, and vice versa. Finally, the passage turns to conditional propositions, where the relationship to the sought conclusion may be directly or by contradiction. In such cases, one must identify what can be extracted (the same or the contradictory) from the conditional, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, and thus complete a conditional syllogism. This is related to the syllogism of reduction (reductio ad absurdum), which is composed of conditionals and can be reduced to a direct syllogism while preserving the same terms. The passage ends with a brief note on acquiring syllogisms for the sought conclusion.

The Inverse Relationship Between Proof and Refutation
Author compares proving/refuting universal and particular propositions via syllogistic forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the principle that the harder a proposition is to prove, the easier it is to refute, and vice versa.

Core Idea
Ease of proof and ease of refutation are inversely related for categorical propositions.

The author observes a symmetrical pattern: if a proposition is difficult to prove (like the universal affirmative), its refutation is relatively easy. Conversely, easy-to-prove propositions (like particular negatives) are hard to refute. This principle has practical implications for argument evaluation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the proof difficulty ranking
From the previous lesson, universal affirmative is hardest to prove; universal negative next; particular propositions (affirmative and negative) are easier to prove because they have more moods.
2
Understand the roles of opposite and contradictory
Refutation can be by opposite (ضد) or by contradictory (نقيض). Opposite uses only one mood; contradictory requires many moods (seven for universals). Particular propositions have no opposite, so only contradictory refutation is possible, which requires more moods.
3
Apply the general rule
The author states: 'Whatever is harder to prove than another, its refutation is easier than the refutation of that other; and whatever is easier to refute than another's refutation, its proof is harder than the proof of that other.'
4
Test with a kalam example
For instance, proving that 'The world is temporally originated' (universal affirmative) is hard; refuting it is easy by showing a single counterexample (contradictory). Proving that 'Some knowledge is acquired' (particular affirmative) is easier; refuting it requires disproving its contradictory (a universal negative) which is harder.
This helps in strategizing during a debate.

The Syllogism

First Steps in Syllogism Analysis
Author presents the need for and first steps of syllogistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first three steps of syllogistic analysis to a given argument: identify premises, assess suitability, and detect conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Analyze premises first; they reveal the argument's worth.

Syllogisms in real discourse are often flawed. To avoid wasting time on infertile arguments, we need a systematic method. The author of our text provides the first steps: start with premises, not terms, and check if they are suitable to the sought conclusion. This lesson guides you through that initial analysis using an Islamic example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogistic statement
Find the argument in the text. It may be distorted, incomplete, or redundant. Focus on the logical core.
2
Extract the premises first
Before looking at the terms (subject/predicate), list the premises. They are fewer and easier to handle. This is the author's priority.
3
Check suitability (munāsaba)
Ask: Does the premise relate to the sought conclusion? If no relation at all (partial or whole), the argument is infertile and can be discarded. If there is a relation, determine if it is total or partial.
4
Identify conditional syllogisms
If a premise suits the conclusion wholly (as identical or contradictory), it is a conditional proposition. Look for an extraction premise that affirms or denies the antecedent/consequent. If present, you have a valid conditional syllogism; if not, the argument is defective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في تحليل الأقيسة والإشارة إلى أمور وتنبيهات معينة في ذلك نقول: إنه لما كانت الأقوال القياسية الموردة في المخاطبات والمكاتبات ربما كانت على القانون المذكور، وربما كانت عقيمة لا تنفع، أو محرفة عن ترتيبها الطبيعي، أو ناقصة مضمرة، أو زائدة زيادة لا تفيد أو أنها متركبة تركيباً موصولاً أو مفصولاً، والإنسان قد لا يستغني بفطرته في معرفة هذه الأقسام دون إعداد قانون يهتدي به إليها، احتجنا إلى إعداده، وذلك هو التحليل، فإنه مهما أعطى حقه في سلوكه كان مؤدياً إلى المطلوب فواجب أن نعرف مدركه على وجه كلي متعلق بصورة القياس، لا من حيث هو برهاني، أو خطابي، أو غير ذلك. فنقول : إذا وجدت قولاً قياسياً وأردت اعتباره، فأول ما يجب البحث فيه عن المقدمات ثم الحدود، إذ المقدمات أقل من الحدود، والبحث عن الأول أيسر، ولأن البحث عند الحدود غير كاف عن البحث عن التأليف المعطي للمطلوب، فإنه ربما كانت غير مؤلفة، وربما كانت مؤلفة تأليفاً غير مجد، أو مجدياً وأصنافه متعددة، وكل منها فيفتقر إلى بحث خاص غير البحث عن نفس الحدود، والبحث المقدمات إذا أدَّى إليها كان ذلك مغنياً عن البحث عن الحدود، فإن وجدت القول مشتملاً على مقدمات فأول ما يجب البحث عنه أنه هل بينها وبين المطلوب مناسبة أم لا؟ فإن لم يكن بينها وبين المطلوب مناسبة لا بجزئه ولا بكله، فإياك والاشتغال به؛ فإنه عقيم. وإن كان منها ما يناسب المطلوب، نظر هل يناسبه بكلّه أو بجزئه، فإن ناسبه بكلّه إما عيناً أو نقيضاً، فالمقدمة شرطية، وعند ذلك فعليك باعتبار اتصالها وانفصالها، ولا بُدّ من مقدمة أخرى استثنائية للعين أو النقيض كما عرف، فإن وجدتها في القول فلا قياس استثنائي، وإن لم تجدها فلا قياس.
Chapter Six: On analysis of syllogisms and reference to matters and helpful warnings therein. We say: Since syllogistic statements presented in speeches and letters are sometimes according to the mentioned law, and sometimes are infertile, beneficial not, or distorted from their natural order, or deficient and implicit, or redundant in a way that does not benefit, or they are composed in a connected or disconnected composition, and man may not be independent by his natural disposition in knowing these types without preparing a law by which he is guided to them, we needed to prepare it. That is analysis, for whenever it is given its due in its practice, it leads to the sought. So it is necessary that we know its method in a universal manner related to the form of the syllogism, not insofar as it is demonstrative, rhetorical, or other. So we say: If you find a syllogistic statement and you want to consider it, the first thing that must be investigated in it is the premises, then the terms, for the premises are fewer than the terms, and investigation of the first is easier, and because investigation of the terms is not sufficient without investigation of the composition that gives the sought, for sometimes they are not composed, and sometimes they are composed in a fruitless composition, or fruitful, and its types are many, and each of them requires a specific investigation other than the investigation of the terms themselves. And investigation of the premises, if it leads to them, suffices from investigation of the terms. If you find the statement containing premises, the first thing to investigate is whether there is suitability between them and the sought or not. If there is no suitability between them and the sought, neither partially nor wholly, then beware of occupying yourself with it; for it is infertile. And if among them there is what suits the sought, consider whether it suits it wholly or partially. If it suits it wholly, either identical or contradictory, then the premise is conditional. And at that point, you must consider its conjunction and disjunction, and there must be another extraction premise for the identical or contradictory as known. If you find it in the statement, then there is a conditional syllogism; if you do not find it, then there is no syllogism.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Six, dedicated to the analysis of syllogisms. The author begins by noting that in actual discourse (speeches and writings), syllogistic statements often deviate from the ideal logical form: they may be useless, distorted, incomplete, redundant, or composed in various ways. Human natural reason is insufficient to handle all these cases without a proper law or method. Therefore, the author presents a method for analysis. The analysis is conducted in a universal manner, regardless of whether the syllogism is demonstrative, rhetorical, or otherwise. The first step is to examine the premises before the terms, because premises are fewer in number and thus easier to analyze. Also, simply examining terms does not reveal whether the composition gives the desired conclusion; the composition itself must be checked. If the statement contains premises, the next step is to determine whether there is suitability (appropriateness) between the premises and the sought conclusion. If there is no suitability at all—neither partial nor whole—the argument is infertile and should be ignored. If there is some suitability, we then determine if it is total or partial. If the premise suits the sought wholly, either as identical (same subject/predicate) or contradictory, then the premise is a conditional proposition (shartiyya). In that case, we must consider whether it is a conjunctive or disjunctive conditional, and we need an additional extraction premise (mustathniyya) for the identical or contradictory, as is well known. If we find that extraction premise in the statement, then we have a valid conditional syllogism; if not, then the statement is not a proper syllogism. The author thus provides a systematic approach to decompose and evaluate arguments found in natural language.

Key Takeaway
The first step in syllogistic analysis is to examine the premises before the terms, checking for suitability with the sought conclusion to identify conditional syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following natural language argument from fiqh: 'If an act causes harm, it is prohibited. This act causes harm. Therefore, it is prohibited.' Apply the author's first steps: identify the premises, check suitability, and state whether a conditional syllogism is present.
?
Find a real argument from a kalam text (e.g., the proof of the huduth) and analyze it using the author's method: list premises, assess suitability, and determine if it is a conditional syllogism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Systematic Analysis of Syllogisms
Author outlines the need for systematic analysis and initial steps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the author's four-step method to analyze any syllogistic statement from a theological or juristic text.

Core Idea
Analysis begins with premises, not terms, and discards fruitless arguments immediately.

The author begins this chapter by observing that syllogistic statements found in real debates and writings can be defective in many ways: incomplete, distorted, redundant, or simply ill-formed. He argues that natural intelligence alone is not enough to detect these defects; a systematic method is needed. In this lesson, you will learn that method and practice it on passages from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Step 1: Locate the premises and terms
When you encounter a syllogistic statement, first identify the premises (al-muqaddimat). The author tells us to start with premises because they are fewer and easier to check than terms. Resist the urge to jump directly to the terms (subject and predicate).
Premises are the statements that are supposed to support the conclusion.
2
Step 2: Check appropriateness between premises and conclusion
Ask: Is there any relationship (munasaba) between each premise and the sought conclusion? The relationship can be partial (the premise relates to only part of the conclusion) or whole (the premise is identical to the conclusion or its contradictory). If no premise relates to the conclusion at all, the argument is fruitless and should be discarded.
The author warns: 'If there is no appropriateness, beware of concerning yourself with it.'
3
Step 3: Classify wholly appropriate premises as conditional
If a premise relates to the conclusion wholly, it must be a conditional statement (shartiyya) — either a conjunctive conditional (muttasila, 'if...then') or a disjunctive conditional (munfasila, 'either...or'). This is because the premise covers the entire conclusion identically or contradictorily.
Example: If the conclusion is 'the world is originated', a wholly appropriate premise would be 'If the world is originated, then it is contingent' (identity) or 'If the world is not originated, then it is eternal' (contradiction).
4
Step 4: Look for the exceptive premise
Once you have identified the conditional premise, you must search for a second premise that is exceptive (istithna'iyya) — that is, one that affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent of the conditional. If this exceptive premise is present, you have a valid exceptive syllogism; if not, there is no syllogism at all.
The exceptive premise is what activates the conditional to yield the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس
في تحليل الأقيسة والإشارة إلى أمور
وتنبيهات معينة في ذلك
نقول: إنه لما كانت الأقوال القياسية الموردة في المخاطبات والمكاتبات ربما كانت على القانون المذكور، وربما كانت عقيمة لا تنفع، أو محرفة عن ترتيبها الطبيعي، أو ناقصة مضمرة، أو زائدة زيادة لا تفيد أو أنها متركبة تركيباً موصولاً أو مفصولاً، والإنسان قد لا يستغني بفطرته في معرفة هذه الأقسام دون إعداد قانون يهتدي به إليها، احتجنا إلى إعداده، وذلك هو التحليل، فإنه مهما أعطى حقه في سلوكه كان مؤدياً إلى المطلوب فواجب أن نعرف مدركه على وجه كلي متعلق بصورة القياس، لا من حيث هو برهاني، أو خطابي، أو غير ذلك. فنقول : إذا وجدت قولاً قياسياً وأردت اعتباره، فأول ما يجب البحث فيه عن المقدمات ثم الحدود، إذ المقدمات أقل من الحدود، والبحث عن الأول أيسر، ولأن البحث عند الحدود غير كاف عن البحث عن التأليف المعطي للمطلوب، فإنه ربما كانت غير مؤلفة، وربما كانت مؤلفة تأليفاً غير مجد، أو مجدياً وأصنافه متعددة، وكل منها فيفتقر إلى بحث خاص غير البحث عن نفس الحدود، والبحث المقدمات إذا أدَّى إليها كان ذلك مغنياً عن البحث عن الحدود، فإن وجدت القول مشتملاً على مقدمات فأول ما يجب البحث عنه أنه هل بينها وبين المطلوب مناسبة أم لا؟ فإن لم يكن بينها وبين المطلوب مناسبة لا بجزئه ولا بكله، فإياك والاشتغال به؛ فإنه عقيم. وإن كان منها ما يناسب المطلوب ،نظر هل يناسبه بكلّه أو بجزئه، فإن ناسبه بكلّه إما عيناً أو نقيضاً، فالمقدمة شرطية، وعند ذلك فعليك باعتبار اتصالها وانفصالها، ولا بُدّ من مقدمة أخرى استثنائية للعين أو النقيض كما عرف، فإن وجدتها في القول فلا قياس استثنائي، وإن لم تجدها فلا قياس.
Chapter Six: On the analysis of syllogisms and indicating some matters and helpful reminders therein. We say: Since the syllogistic statements used in conversations and correspondences may sometimes be according to the mentioned [sound] form, and sometimes they may be fruitless, or distorted from their natural order, or deficient with implied [premises], or with superfluous additions that do not benefit, or they may be composed in a connected or disconnected manner, and man may not be independent with his natural disposition in knowing these divisions without preparing a rule by which he can be guided to them, we needed to prepare it, and that is the analysis. For whenever it is given its proper due in its practice, it leads to the sought result. So it is necessary that we know its method in a universal way related to the form of the syllogism, not in terms of it being demonstrative, or rhetorical, or otherwise. So we say: If you find a syllogistic statement and you wish to examine it, the first thing you must investigate in it is the premises, then the terms, because the premises are fewer than the terms, and the investigation of the former is easier, and because the investigation of the terms is not sufficient for the investigation of the composition that gives the sought result, for indeed sometimes they are not composed, and sometimes they are composed in a useless composition, or a beneficial one, and its types are many, and each of them needs a special investigation other than the investigation of the terms themselves. And the investigation of the premises, if it leads to them, suffices for the investigation of the terms. So if you find the statement containing premises, the first thing that must be investigated is whether there is an appropriateness between them and the sought conclusion or not. For if there is no appropriateness between them and the sought conclusion, neither partially nor wholly, then beware of concerning yourself with it, for it is fruitless. And if among them there is something appropriate to the sought conclusion, then see whether it is appropriate to it wholly or partially. If it is appropriate to it wholly, either as identical or as contradictory, then the premise is conditional. And then you must consider its conjunction and disjunction, and there must be another premise that is exceptive for the identical or contradictory as known. If you find it in the statement, then there is an exceptive syllogism; if you do not find it, then there is no syllogism.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the analysis (tahlil) of syllogisms. It begins by stating the various ways syllogistic statements can appear: sound, fruitless, distorted, incomplete, redundant, or composed in different ways. The author argues that unaided human nature is insufficient to recognize these divisions without a systematic rule, hence the need for analysis. The method is universal, applicable to all types of syllogisms (demonstrative, rhetorical, etc.).

The author then prescribes the order of investigation: first examine the premises, then the terms. This is because premises are fewer and easier to check, and because examining terms alone does not reveal whether the composition yields the conclusion. If the statement contains premises, the next step is to check whether they are appropriate to the conclusion. If not wholly or partially appropriate, the syllogism is fruitless and should be discarded.

If some premise is appropriate, one must determine whether it is appropriate wholly or partially. If wholly appropriate—either identical to the conclusion or its contradictory—then the premise is conditional (shartiyya). In such a case, one must look for another premise that is exceptive (istithna'iyya) corresponding to the identity or contradiction. If found, the syllogism is an exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i); if not, there is no syllogism at all.

Note: All examples should come from the Islamic sciences. For instance, if the conclusion is "this nabidh is haram," a wholly appropriate premise might be "every intoxicant is haram" (identical) or "no non-intoxicant is haram" (contradictory).

The Exception for Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Exception for compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a compound conditional syllogism yields a conclusion without an explicit intermediate step, using the example provided.

Core Idea
Composition allows direct conclusion without showing every intermediate step.

In the analysis of compound syllogisms, there is a special case where the conclusion follows immediately from the premises due to composition, without needing to state the intermediate exceptive syllogism. This lesson examines that exception using a concrete example with abstract variables, which we will then map to an Islamic science example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound conditional argument
Locate the two conditional premises and the exceptive statement. In the example: 'Everything that is B is J) then (D is R)' and 'Everything that is D is R) then A is H', with the exception 'but not A is H'.
2
See the implicit intermediate step
Normally, from the second conditional and exception you would derive 'not D is R' by modus tollens, then from the first conditional and that result derive 'not B is J'. The author notes that this explicit enumeration is unnecessary due to composition.
3
Map the variables to Islamic science
Replace variables: B = intoxicant, J = prohibited, D = khamr, R = intoxicant, A = nabidh, H = permissible. The argument becomes: 'If everything intoxicant is prohibited, then khamr is intoxicant; if khamr is intoxicant, then nabidh is permissible; but nabidh is not permissible; therefore, not everything intoxicant is prohibited.' This shows the exception's power.
4
Understand why the exception holds
The result of the exception is already contained in the composition of the conditionals. The author says: 'because the exception's result stands in it, so it sufficed with mentioning its syllogism for it.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللهم إلا أن يكون ثم تركيب كما يقال مثلاً: كل ما كان "ب" "ج") فـ ("د" "ر") و(كل ما كان "د" "ر") فـ "أ" "هـ" لكن ليس "أ" "هـ" فإنه ينتج (ليس "ب" "ج") وإن لم يكن الاستثناء لنقيض تاليها ولا غير مقدمها في القول، وليس ذلك إلا لما فيه من التركيب وإن الاستثناء وقفت نتيجته فيه فاكتفى بذكر قياسها عنها، وإلا كان الواجب أن يقال: (لكن ليس "أ" "هـ") فـ (ليس "د" "ر") فـ (ليس "ب" "ج")، وعند ذلك فتحليل ما مثل هذا القياس أن تنظر بعد معرفتك للمقدمة المناسبة للمطلوب وهي (كل ما كان "ب" "ج") فـ ("د" "ر") وتجعل استثناء نقيض التالي منها مطلوباً، ثم تنظر فإن وجدت ما يناسبه على النحو المفصل أو لا، فإما أن يناسبه بكليته أو بجزئه، فإن كان بكليته فالمقدمة أيضاً شرطية إما متصلة أو منفصلة، فعليك بالبحث مقدمة أخرى تكون هي الاستثنائية، بحيث يكون لزومه عنهما، وهكذا لا تزال إلى أن تنتهى على جميع أجزاء القول، فإن وجدت ما تنتهي معرفة الاستثنائية إليه فقد تم القياس من الشرطيات كما مضى، وإلا فلا يكون الأمر إن كان البحث عن معرفة استثنائية عن المقدمة أيضاً. وأما إن كانت مناسبة له بجزئه فلا بُدّ من مقدمة أخرى تناسبه بالجزء الآخر، وإلا فلا قياس، وعند الظفر بها فلا بُدّ من حد أوسط يكون مشتركاً بينهما، فإن ظهر الحد الأوسط فالقياس اقتراني، كان بسيطاً أو مركباً، وعليك باعتباره من أي شكل هو، وأي المقدمتين هي الصغرى، وأيهما هي الكبرى، وتعرف به ما تجب معرفته كما مضى. وإن لم تظفر بالحد الأوسط فإما أن يكون القول من مقدمتين فقط أو أكثر من ذلك، فإن كان الأول فلا قياس، وإن كان الثاني بأن كان مؤلفاً من ثلاث مقدمات مثلاً، كما لو كان المطلوب كل "ج" "ب") وكان القول القياسي (كل "ج" "أ") و(كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "د" "ب") فالواجب أن تنظر في ما يناسب أحد الحديـن مـن المقدمات وهي مثلاً ( كل " " "أ")، ثم تنظر فإن كان من المقدمات ما يشاركها في "ج" حد أوسط وإلا فلا قياس وإن كان كما في قولنا: ((كل "أ" "د") فتنظر فإن كانت المقدمة الأخرى تشارك هذه في حد أوسط وإلا فلا قياس أيضاً، وإن كانت مشاركة
Unless there is a composition, as one says for example: 'Everything that is B is J) then (D is R) and (Everything that is D is R) then A is H but not A is H', then it produces (not B is J) even if the exception is not for the contradictory of its consequent nor for the antecedent in the statement. And that is only because of the composition in it, and because the exception's result stands in it, so it sufficed with mentioning its syllogism for it. Otherwise it would have been necessary to say: 'But not A is H, so not D is R, so not B is J.' And at that, the analysis of such a syllogism is that you look, after your knowledge of the premise appropriate to the sought conclusion, which is (Everything that is B is J) then (D is R), and you make the exception of the contradictory of its consequent a sought [conclusion]. Then you see whether you find what is appropriate to it in the detailed manner or not. Either it is appropriate to it wholly or partially. If it is wholly, then the premise is also conditional, either conjunctive or disjunctive. Then you must search for another premise that is the exceptive one, such that its implication is from them. And so on until you finish all parts of the statement. If you find that the knowledge of the exceptive premise ends at it, then the syllogism from conditionals is completed as before. Otherwise, it is not the case if the search for an exceptive premise from the premise also [fails]. And if it is appropriate to it partially, then there must be another premise that is appropriate to the other part, otherwise there is no syllogism. And upon obtaining it, there must be a middle term common between them. If the middle term appears, then the syllogism is conjunctive (iqtirani), whether simple or compound. And you must consider which figure it is, and which of the two premises is the minor, and which is the major, and you know by it what must be known as before. And if you do not obtain the middle term, then either the statement is of two premises only or more than that. If the former, then no syllogism. If the latter, by being composed of three premises for example, as if the sought is 'Every J is B' and the syllogistic statement is 'Every J is A' and 'Every A is J' and 'Every D is B', then you must look at what is appropriate to one of the two limits from the premises, which is for example (Every [ ] is A) [text corrupted], then see if among the premises there is something that shares with it a middle term; otherwise no syllogism. And if it is as in our saying: 'Every A is D', then see if the other premise shares with this one a middle term; otherwise also no syllogism. And if it shares...
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage deals with an exception in the analysis of compound syllogisms. The author presents a scenario where a syllogism composed of two conditionals allows an immediate conclusion without explicitly stating the intermediate exceptive premise. The example uses variables: 'Everything that is B is J) then (D is R)' and 'Everything that is D is R) then A is H', with the exception 'but not A is H'. This yields the conclusion 'not B is J' directly, even though the exception is not for the consequent of the first conditional. The reason is the composition (tarkib) itself; the result of the exception is already contained in the composition, so mentioning the intermediate step is unnecessary.

The author then generalizes the analytical procedure: first identify the premise appropriate to the conclusion, then treat the exception of the contradictory of its consequent as a new sought conclusion. Then check if this new sought is itself appropriate to some premise, either wholly or partially. If wholly, that premise is again conditional, requiring another exceptive premise, and so on until the chain ends. If partially, a second premise is needed, and then a middle term must be found to combine them in a conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani). The middle term determines the figure and which premise is minor or major.

If no middle term is found, the syllogism may be invalid unless it has more than two premises. The author gives an example with three premises: 'Every J is A', 'Every A is J', and 'Every D is B', with the conclusion 'Every J is B'. The method is to find a premise that contains one of the terms of the conclusion and then see if another premise shares a middle term with it.

Note: All examples should be from Islamic sciences. For instance, in fiqh, one could say: 'Everything that is khamr is haram' and 'Everything that is haram is prohibited' leads to 'This nabidh is prohibited' if it is khamr. The composition example could analogize to chain arguments in usul al-fiqh.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism can yield a conclusion directly due to composition, without showing every intermediate step.
